Sir Chris Bryant MP - Balancing act: Britain walking the trade tightrope

Chatham House(89.4K) ·33:21 ·288 views · 2026-03-20
Sir Chris Bryant MP (Minister for Trade, Department for Business and Trade)
> "Promote what we can, protect what we must.
TL;DR: Sir Chris Bryant MP advocates for a proactive, "free and fair" trade strategy that rejects protectionist retreats while selectively protecting critical sectors like steel to ensure national security and economic resilience. Rejecting Binary Choices: Bryant argues the UK...

TL;DR: Sir Chris Bryant MP advocates for a proactive, "free and fair" trade strategy that rejects protectionist retreats while selectively protecting critical sectors like steel to ensure national security and economic resilience.

Redefining the "Trade Tightrope" Philosophy

  • Rejecting Binary Choices: Bryant argues the UK does not have to choose between the US, EU, or China. Trade is not a "push me pull you" act; the goal is strong relations with all partners.
  • The "Promote and Protect" Doctrine: The UK's strategy is to promote trade where possible and protect sectors when necessary.
      • Steel Sector: Protective measures were implemented to counter global overcapacity and unfair subsidies. UK steel production has plummeted from 27.8 million tons in 1970 to just 4 million in 2024.
      • National Security: Domestic steel is deemed essential for increasing defense expenditure and infrastructure projects (1.5 million new homes).
  • Low Center of Gravity: Bryant emphasizes that the UK must remain anchored in its values—absolute commitment to free trade—while addressing modern challenges like slavery, dumping, and environmental degradation.

Revitalizing the Multilateral Trading System

  • WTO Reform: A top priority for the upcoming MC14 is creating a more relevant, flexible, and accessible World Trade Organization. Bryant warns that if the WTO fades into irrelevance, the rules-based order will collapse.
  • Combatting "Somnambulism": Bryant criticizes the "laboriously slow" pace of international trade negotiations. He warns against "somnambulism" (sleepwalking), noting that it can take a year just to draw up a mandate before negotiations even begin.
  • Addressing Protectionism: Between 2015 and 2023, the number of protectionist measures globally more than quadrupled. Bryant argues this makes the case for free trade more urgent, not less.

Strategic Partnerships: EU, CPTPP, and Beyond

  • The EU Relationship: The government aims for "frictionless trade" with the EU.
      • Immediate Goals: Securing an SPS (Sanitary and Phytosanitary) deal, mutual recognition of professional standards, and easing business travel.
      • Specific Impacts: Bryant highlighted that 80% of UK flowers come through the Netherlands; current bureaucratic hurdles led to significantly higher flower prices for UK consumers compared to Europe.
  • CPTPP Expansion: The UK views the Comprehensive and Progressive Agreement for Trans-Pacific Partnership as a "force for predictability."
      • Membership Growth: Bryant is pushing for the rapid accession of Costa Rica, UAE, Indonesia, and the Philippines.
      • Global Weight: Including the EU in supply chain dialogues could bring CPTPP-aligned trade to 35% of the global total.
  • African Partnerships: Currently focused on country-by-country agreements (Nigeria, South Africa). Recent deals include a £70 million UK Export Finance agreement for Nigerian ports using British steel.

The Shift to Services and the "Experience Economy"

  • The Third Industrial Revolution: Bryant identifies a shift from manufactured goods to services, and now to "experiences."
  • Creative Industries Superpower: The creative sector accounts for 15% of UK services exports and grew by 5% last year.
  • Digital Trade Infrastructure: The UK is pushing to include digital trade and services in all new Free Trade Agreements (FTAs), as the current international rules for digital commerce remain "thin on the ground."
  • Economic Security: There is a critical need to diversify the processing of critical minerals. Currently, 70-90% of processing is concentrated in a single country (China), creating a strategic vulnerability the UK intends to address through plurilateral agreements.

Notable Quotes

"Promote what we can, protect what we must. They [trade remedies] don't undermine free trade. In fact... they are specifically designed to bolster free trade." — Sir Chris Bryant MP
"The real danger for the UK, I believe as trade minister, is not funambulism [tightrope walking], it's somnambulism [sleepwalking]. We can be laboriously slow." — Sir Chris Bryant MP
"It is often the case that one generation believes something, the next generation assumes it, and the third generation forgets it. In the absence of a good case for free trade 30 years later, rather than trade barriers coming down, we've seen more of them go up." — Sir Chris Bryant MP
"I want us to get to frictionless trade... I want us to be utterly, utterly ambitious in this regard." — Sir Chris Bryant MP
Great, well hello again. We're now into our next session which is titled Balancing Act, Britain walking the trade tightrope. And to address this topic we are very fortunate to have with us Sir Chris Bryant, who's the UK trade minister and Minister of State at the Department of Business and Trade. So welcome Minister of State, thank you very much for being with us. The session first of all is on the record. We will first of all have some initial comments from the minister and then we'll go into a Q&A and there will be an opportunity for questions from the audience. So Minister, over to you. Well thank you very much. Not just a few comments but a proper formal speech and everything. So good afternoon everybody, it's a delight to be here. I want to start by slightly taking issue with the title of this session, because I'm awkward like that, which is 'Britain walking the trade tightrope'. I suppose the implication is that we're engaged in a balancing act between the US and the EU. Or that our trade policy is a high-wire act, a dangerous risk in today's climate. Or that we're navigating a narrow strait between an American Scylla and a Chinese Charybdis and that if we sail too close to either we risk ruin. I don't accept this characterization of the challenge ahead. I don't think we have to choose between the competing demands of different trading partners. The EU is our biggest trading partner. The US is our biggest single country for exports and imports. And I defy anyone in the room to spend an hour today without any Chinese product. Trade isn't a 'push me pull you'. That's a reference to a film from my childhood and your childhood as well clearly. We need strong trading relations with all our partners. And I'm delighted to see our exports reach £926 billion last year. But I do want to go with the tightrope metaphor for a moment because I'm told the key to tightrope walking is to maintain a low center of gravity and focus your gaze on the end of the rope rather than your feet. We'll be trying this later. When it comes to trade, I think that means two things. Firstly, a low center of gravity means sticking to our values. In our case that means a commitment, an absolute commitment, to the principle of free and fair trade. We have always prospered best as a nation that is open to two-way trade. It is no accident that at the very heart of the House of Lords lies the woolsack, a symbol of Britain's early wealth that came from the wool trade, our key Middle Ages export. And over the centuries we have sought out new markets, bringing in spices, silk and porcelain, tobacco and potatoes, mangoes and mange-tout too. And perhaps more than most, our modern economy is based on give and take, endlessly exporting and importing. A British car for instance is likely to include components from many countries. Just like a French-built Renault will include British electronics and braking systems. Or a European Airbus plane will include British wings and engines, making it 30% British. So, we more than most need to be proud beacons of free trade. If there are sirens beguiling us to perdition on the rocks, they are the arch-protectionists who would make us retreat into narrow nationalism. But I would add that it also has to be fair trade. Modern slavery, dumping, environmental degradation, deliberate anti-competitive subsidies, these all challenge free trade. And it is only right that countries like the UK take measures to protect key national industrial sectors like steel when they are threatened by global overcapacity. Hence the measures announced alongside our steel strategy this morning. That's not a sign, I want to make this very clear, that's not a sign of a shift in our philosophy away from free trade. It's a reassertion of the principle of free and fair trade. Steel is a critical sector for the UK, especially at a time when defense expenditure needs to rise. We needed to take action to preserve and enhance our domestic sector after years of deliberate global overcapacity, unfair subsidy and other protectionist measures had whittled it down from 27.8 million tons in 1970 to just 4 million in 2024. These measures are a reflection of our overall trade strategy. Promote what we can, protect what we must. They don't undermine free trade. In fact, all the work we do to tackle unfair measures around the world, for instance through the Trade Remedies Authority, are specifically designed to bolster free trade because whatever our political hue, the UK will always fight for free trade. Of course, free and fair trade isn't just about trade remedies. It also provides opportunities for other nations, particularly developing countries, to grow their own economies. It's a simple fact that as lower and middle income economies increased their share in exports, poverty in those nations went down. That's good for the world and good for the United Kingdom. And good for a socialist like me. So, it's time we waved the banner of free and fair trade more enthusiastically. I know people look at me with suspicion when I say that. Free trade? Really? Still? Even today? I understand that suspicion. After all, it feels when you look at the global trading landscape that free trade is in retreat. We've seen nations disregard the rules, distort markets, and use trade to pressurize their neighbors. WTO members have regularly failed to be open and transparent about their state subsidies. And between 2015 and 2023 the number of protectionist measures around the world had more than quadrupled. Populists everywhere proclaim the importance of protectionism. But that doesn't mean free trade is the wrong approach. It just means it's even more important that we fight for it. Because for a long time we have taken free trade for granted. When the WTO was set up over 30 years ago, well set up earlier and reinforced with the GATT treaties, we assumed the war for free trade was won. That the debate was settled and that in the future barriers would wither and collapse in the face of an obviously superior philosophy of free trade. And so we stopped making the argument. But unfortunately it is often the case that one generation believes something, the next generation assumes it and the third generation forgets it. In the absence of a good case for free trade 30 years later, rather than trade barriers coming down we've seen more of them go up. And we must challenge that trend because free trade is what's best for all of us. But it only works if everyone signs up to it. And that means there must be rules, principles and boundaries that everyone agrees to work within and a strong sanction regime to make sure those boundaries are strong. That's why we need a strong World Trade Organization. Without it the multilateral trading system that we have all enjoyed will fail. True, in the 31 years since the WTO was formed, the world has changed and the WTO needs to adapt and reform so it can continue to safeguard free and fair trade. That's why our top priority at MC14 next week is to lay out a vision for a WTO that is more relevant, more flexible and more accessible. We need a WTO that works. A WTO that works now. And a WTO that works for everyone. And we're going to MC14 to lay the groundwork to make that happen and deliver change by MC15. Global trade has suffered some quakes in recent years. Between COVID, the blocking of the Suez Canal, Ukraine, attacks in the Red Sea and of course the current situation in the Straits of Hormuz, we've seen crisis overlapping crisis, all of which has shaken the rules-based order. That's the only time I'm using that phrase in this speech. But if the WTO were to collapse or even fade into irrelevance, that would bring the whole thing crashing down. So yes, despite the rise in global protectionism, despite economic coercion and despite a more complicated world, we remain committed to a strong WTO and to free and fair trade. As I said earlier, that's all part of sticking to our values, or to pursue the tightrope metaphor, keeping our center of gravity low. Which takes me to my second point. Keeping our eyes on the end of the rope rather than staring at our feet every step of the way. The truth is we tend to approach too many trade issues one at a time, line by line, step by step. That especially applies to our relationship with the European Union. But our trading posture in the world isn't a question of one policy after another. It's a much bigger existential question. Do we subscribe to a 'you in your small corner and me in mine' approach, as the old schoolboy hymn went? Do we think of our economy as hermetically sealed? Or do we commit ourselves to a wholehearted passion for free and fair trade? The evidence of history suggests that when General Franco tried autarky in Spain, it nearly bankrupted the country. And it's the total of mutual trade that matters, not the balance of trade with individual countries. Imports of course keep costs down. Let me end with another distinction drawing on the tightrope metaphor. The French for tightrope walker is funambule. Funambulist. The real danger for the UK, I believe as trade minister, is not funambulism, it's somnambulism. We can be laboriously slow. By the time we decide to look at a new FTA and draw up a mandate, a whole 12 months will have passed. And that's before we start negotiations which can go on for years. We can be too finickity too. Of course we have to approach all our trading relationships with our eyes wide open, but we need to act with a sense of urgency, determination and drive. That doesn't mean we have to throw all the cards up in the air and hope they land well. We need to work within the structures and the strictures that aim to provide a global level playing field. But we can't be hanging about on the tightrope. Thank you. Well Minister, thank you very much. I just think the vast majority of people in this room agree with almost everything you've said. Hurrah, I can go! No, however, looking across the Atlantic, I doubt President Trump agrees with almost anything you've said. Now, you know, that's a fact of life and last year almost a year ago we did a trade deal with the US. I think arguably it was the best deal that we could do at the time. Can you say what, you know, what's the status of that deal now particularly after the Supreme Court ruling? And shouldn't we now be trying to get a better deal? I mean it was an asymmetric deal, President Trump put tariffs on us, we had to some extent reduce our protections against the US. Couldn't we get a better deal now? Actually, sorry, there is one other thing I'd say. That won't work. Charlie Boyle, NVIDIA | NVIDIA GTC '26

Charlie Boyle (Vice President of DGX, NVIDIA)

TL;DR NVIDIA is transitioning from providing individual chips to delivering full-scale "AI Factories" powered by the Vera-Rubin platform, which offers a 35x performance increase over Blackwell to radically lower the cost of "token economics." This shift enables a new "agentic"...

TL;DR

NVIDIA is transitioning from providing individual chips to delivering full-scale "AI Factories" powered by the Vera-Rubin platform, which offers a 35x performance increase over Blackwell to radically lower the cost of "token economics." This shift enables a new "agentic" era where autonomous AI agents drive business revenue, transforming technology infrastructure from a capital expense into a direct revenue generator.

The Vera-Rubin Platform and the 12-Month Cadence

  • Accelerated Innovation Cycle: NVIDIA has committed to a strict 12-month release cadence, moving from a transistor-density focus to extreme hardware-software co-design.
      • Performance Jumps: The Vera-Rubin platform is projected to be 35x faster than Grace Blackwell; Boyle noted that while critics doubted previous 35x claims, independent tests actually showed 50x improvements due to software optimization.
  • The Role of DGX: Marking the 10th anniversary of DGX, NVIDIA continues to use these systems as vertically integrated "reference designs" that allow partners like Dell and Supermicro to ship compatible systems on day one.
  • Software Longevity: Despite the rapid hardware cycles, the software remains compatible. Applications written for the original DGX-1 in 2016 still run on the Rubin platform today, protecting customer investments in the CUDA ecosystem.

The Rise of Agentic AI and NIM

  • NVIDIA Inference Microservices (NIM): The "Open NIM" ecosystem allows users to build functional AI agents using natural language prompts, lowering the barrier to entry for software development.
  • The Agentic Workflow: Future workflows will involve a "General Contractor" agent spawning specialized sub-agents.
      • Infrastructure Requirements: These agents require machine-speed data creation, ingestion, and destruction, necessitating a "sandbox" environment with high-speed memory and storage.
  • Democratizing Innovation: Boyle emphasizes that ideas previously discarded due to development costs can now be prototyped instantly via NIM, allowing one small idea to pivot the direction of an entire business.

Reimagining Infrastructure: Fabric and Storage

  • The Fabric-First Approach: NVIDIA views the network fabric (InfiniBand and Spectrum-X Ethernet) as the critical connector that allows any resource in an AI factory to access any other resource.
  • STX Storage Architecture: NVIDIA is introducing a new reference architecture for storage that places "Vera" AI-capable processors directly into storage boxes.
      • Bypassing Bottlenecks: By using BlueField-4 DPUs and CX-9 NICs, the system offloads data movement tasks from the CPU, allowing agents to process data closer to where it physically resides.
      • Security Integration: This architecture allows existing IT security and access control lists (ACLs) to be "plumbed" directly into AI models, ensuring agents respect long-standing data permissions.

Data Center Efficiency and "Token Economics"

  • Maximizing Power Utilization: Traditional data centers often leave 40% of their provisioned power unused due to safety margins.
      • Chip-Level Controls: The Vera-Rubin architecture uses hardware and software controls to react at speeds humans cannot, allowing data centers to run at 100% power utilization safely.
  • Lowering Unit Costs: By utilizing every watt, companies can fit up to 40% more GPUs in the same power footprint, drastically reducing the cost per token.
  • Capex as a Revenue Driver: The business model has shifted; CEOs now view infrastructure spending as a positive indicator.
      • The "Spend More, Save More" Model: In the AI era, every dollar of Capex spent on tokens and agents correlates to direct revenue growth and improved customer experiences, rather than just "classic IT refresh" costs.
"The biggest takeaway is the Vera-Rubin platform... working together in an AI factory to dramatically lower the cost of delivering tokens. Because the more tokens you have, the more business you can create." — Charlie Boyle
"We are going to be able, with Vera-Rubin... to hit that power limit, allowing you to use every single watt you pay for to generate tokens... you can now get 40% more GPUs in a data center for the same power payment." — Charlie Boyle
"It used to be every dollar I spent on Capex was like, 'Oh, I’ve got to do a refresh.' It’s no longer that—we’ve broken that model. People want to spend more because they know when they do that, their business is going to change." — Charlie Boyle
Hi everyone, welcome to theCUBE's special coverage on the ground here at GTC San Jose 2024. My name is Dave Vellante and I'm here with Charlie Boyle, who's the vice president of DGX at NVIDIA. Big week, Charlie. Congratulations on all the announcements and the innovation. For people who weren't able to watch all the coverage, what's the most important takeaway that you want to leave people with from GTC? You know, I think the biggest takeaway is the Vera-Rubin platform and all the new racks that we've announced. All that is working together in an AI factory to dramatically lower the cost of delivering tokens. Because the more tokens that you have, the more business you can create and the more opportunities you can have out there. With this explosion of the NIM ecosystem, the new applications that you could only ever imagine, I can now build. Now with the power of Vera-Rubin and Vera itself, I can deliver those as a business to drive brand new sources of revenue that were never possible before. You know, I think obviously everybody's familiar with Moore's Law, but I don't think people appreciate the trajectory of the curve that we're on now. You're basically delivering orders of magnitude improvement in performance literally every year. Is that right? How do you do that? How should we be thinking about the importance of that going forward? I mean, it's totally accurate. It is just a testament to not only the great hardware designers that we have, but it's a ton of software work. Part of that, as we said, is that Vera-Rubin is going to be 35x faster than Grace Blackwell, and we fully believe that. Even in the keynote, we were talking about when Jensen put up 35x last year and everyone said, "You're never going to get that." Independent tests showed we were 50x. Part of that is because of the software innovation; we get better every year. That 35x allows customers and companies to do things they couldn't do before. If you can think it, you can build it. Now with open NIM, just you and I having an English conversation to a prompt can build an agent to get real work done for us. That real work can turn into innovation, new products, and new revenue for folks. So we've gone from sort of density on a transistor on a chip to extreme co-design. Is that really the enabler that allows you to be on this 12-month cadence? It is. I mean, it's an absolutely enormous lift. Jensen a few years ago said we're going to go on a one-year cadence and everyone was like, "Are you sure?" We did it, we do it every year, and we're going to keep doing it every year. A big part of that is because the software's compatible. You know, this is the 20-year anniversary of CUDA and the 10-year anniversary of DGX. Software that was running on that original DGX-1 back in 2016 runs on Rubin today. It's that compatibility, that software layer, that allows us to stay on that 12-month cycle. As a developer, you just intersect what system you have in that generation and you know it's going to be faster. It's going to drive down your cost, but your software's the same. Of course you can take advantage of new software capabilities, but that allows us to say, "I'm going to invest in this platform." The stuff I did two years ago still works, and the stuff I'm going to do two years from now is still going to work. Why are you so excited at NVIDIA, generally Jensen specifically, about open NIM? Peter Steinberger was on a Lex Fridman podcast, three hours of going deep as to how he did it, and it's just absolutely remarkable. But why is that so exciting to you and what should people know about it? You know, so all of us have been in technology business for a lot of years, and I'm sure a lot of your audience has too. Every single person, I'm sure viewing this, has had an idea: "I wish I had an application that did X, I wish I had something that did Y." But most of us as business leaders, even ones that have software developers working for us, look at that and go, "Oh, I'm not going to bother them, it's going to take a while." But now with a thought, I could say, "Oh, I'm going to ask the NIM to do something for me, build me something." That may change the way I work. Now, I may ask it to build me like a report or something. We build and ship systems all over the world, and I can say, "Analyze a bunch of this data for me." But then I might show that to my colleagues and they say, "Well, hey, everyone in sales and marketing needs that." So now I need to scale it. That one little thing that I came up with, I still need software developers because now I've got to optimize that. I've got to drive the cost down and drive the token economics up on that. It just makes it possible for ideas that you thought of and threw away because you're like, "I don't know if it's going to work." Now I can try that idea. That one idea could change the direction of your business. You know, NVIDIA's always kind of been a household name, Charlie, because as Jensen said, we as parents funded our kids to get fast GPUs for their gaming. But take us back 10 years to the launch of DGX when you first started with the company. What's that journey been like and help the audience understand where you've come from and where you're going? How did you get here? Yeah, and you know, I'm not that old. It's the 20th anniversary of CUDA and the 10th anniversary of DGX. In this very San Jose Convention Center 10 years ago, we showed the world the first DGX-1. It was behind velvet ropes because we didn't want anyone to touch it. Back then, 10 years ago—and it's funny—the number one customer question was, "What could I possibly need eight GPUs for?" We listen to that now and we're like, "That's hysterical." Like, you don't hire a software developer unless it's like, "Hey, I've got to have 100 GPUs." But the vision then and the reason we built it then is still the reason that we're building DGX now and our systems. It's to take the latest NVIDIA technology, build a vertically integrated stack on that, make that easy to use for customers, and share that with all of our partners as a reference design so they can expand and scale with us out there. It's true today. You know, back then it took our partners a year or more to get to market. Now every single partner that we have is time-to-market; the same day that we're shipping software, they're shipping systems. And that's what Jensen was talking about. All of our partners are building gigawatts worth of infrastructure rolling off their factory lines every single month. What's the enabler there? You're very obviously transparent about your roadmap. You talk about how you're vertically integrated but also horizontally open. Explain that. So part of that vertically integrated piece is you have to build it all together so you know it works. You know, one of the things that you never see behind the scenes: before we ship one Vera-Rubin rack, Jensen has invested billions of dollars for us to build our own clusters internally for all of our engineers. They beat on that and they develop software on that, so that day one when we ship that first production rack—when Dell ships that first production rack, when Supermicro does—we know it works. That extreme co-design, and all the engineers having access to all that infrastructure, building and testing at scale with networking, fabric, and storage means that when we tell you we can get you an AI factory in three weeks or a month, it's because we've already built it. We know the design and we know the recipe. Then it's just humans putting things in data centers at that point and running tests. Mellanox may turn out to be one of the greatest, if not the greatest, acquisition in history. Jensen feels that way, yes, and I feel that way too. I've seen many of them, and I won't bore you with all my top 10, but I think I would put Mellanox right up there. Can you explain the importance of that acquisition generally, but specifically the fabric and how you've evolved the fabric? And so fabric is so important. Funny enough, the original DGX-1s had Mellanox in them. The thing we shipped in 2016 had four InfiniBand cards in it, and then it went up to eight and everything. Back in 2016 and 2017, nobody was clustering anything. But in 2018 and 2019, when we started having the first SuperPOD, we always believed in fabric. We always want any application and any resource you have to be able to get to anything else in your AI factory. That's the power of fabric. The thing is, almost every developer that's developing on an NVIDIA GPU today, whether it's on-prem or in the cloud, is using a fantastically powerful fabric that they never see. It's the NVIDIA software, it's CUDA, it's NCCL, and all of those things that optimize and take advantage of the fabric, but you never see it. That's the power. The best fabric out there is giving you the power that you need, but it gets out of your way to let you do the work that you want. That's why Mellanox has been great. We recently added not only InfiniBand, but we have Spectrum-X so that we can scale out an enormous AI ethernet data center. That's what next-generation fabrics are getting built on. So in 2023 and 2024, we kind of went from request-retrieve chatbots, then reasoning came in, which really kind of drove demand. Now we're going into the agentic age. Multiple agents taking action. What does that mean from an infrastructure standpoint? What do you have to do to design to serve that and then what does it mean ultimately for the end customer? Yeah, you know, I've been thinking a lot about what analogies are like for these new types of agents. I just got done building a house a few years ago. That first request, that first idea that you had, kind of creates your general contractor agent. But then that agent has to spawn sub-agents and specialty agents. They all need access to memory, they all need storage, and they all need a sandbox. We talked a lot about safety on that same AI factory; you may be running thousands of agents. You want to know that your agent is running securely, that my agent's running securely, and then unless we explicitly agree that we're going to talk to each other, that all of our data is separate. Now that used to be humans. Humans used to create data; we create files and save files. But now agents are creating that data, quickly creating, quickly storing, and then also quickly destroying it. So not only do we need the agent, the general contractor agent, all the sub-agents, and all the security, but we need the storage and the data paths on that to support that machine-speed creation, ingestion, monitoring, and destruction of data. So you guys nailed the compute obviously, the InfiniBand piece of it, and Spectrum-X. You've got scale up, you've got scale out, and you've got scale across. Now you've taken another step and gone deeper into the stack with storage. You've sort of reimagined storage and the storage hierarchy. You've got a new reference architecture. You're not competing with the storage industry, you're not selling storage per se, but you're enabling the industry to think differently about how storage should work in an accelerated computing environment. Explain what you've done and I'd like to ask you a few questions about what the implications are for the future. Sure. And you know, I think you hit some of the highlights there. As we think about the agentic workflow, all the storage partners that you saw supporting the release—I started talking to them about STX kind of end of last year—they're all in. Because this doesn't replace anything we're doing in system of record, doesn't replace high-performance storage, and doesn't replace object storage. It's about what an agent needs to do with storage. Part of that is just like many things; Jensen loves to invest in something that he thinks is going to be big. That's why I'm actually the lead of that program for NVIDIA, which is part of the reason we're talking. But as he thought about it, he said, "You know, we need to build one reference. So in that storage box, not only do you have drives, not only do you have CX-9 and BlueField, but you have Vera. That enables that agentic workflow because it's the same Vera and the same agents, but now we're moving a lot of that processing a lot closer to the physical data. That's why all of our storage partners are super excited about it because now they can take their stack and put it on that brand new reference architecture. They didn't have to do the R&D on it; we're doing it. As we said, agents and NIM are all about security. Well, all of our storage partners have been securing access control lists and all that information already exists in their storage systems. Now as a developer, I don't have to go re-ask for all the permissions that IT has already given me over the last 10 years. So with the trajectory that you're on with GPUs, networking became the bottleneck, and you addressed that. Storage then became the bottleneck, and you kind of reimagined that. You got BlueField-4 DPUs to allow you to offload data movement tasks, which normally was the CPU, so that created the historical bottleneck. You've got Spectrum-X Ethernet, you've got your SuperNICs, and you're now being able to bypass bottlenecks and go right to RDMA. You've got sort of a reference architecture for how you scale KV cache at rack scale. All of that is new and as you say the ecosystem is sort of leaning into it. What does that allow us to do that we wouldn't be able to do previously? You know, a big part of it is the big jump even between Blackwell and this generation. Blackwell, when we thought about an AI factory, was just how many Blackwell racks you have. This generation, we've created a number of new rack architectures. We talked about the Vera rack because you need a lot more CPU for your agentic workflow. We talked about the storage rack and we talked about the networking. What Vera-Rubin and that whole multi-rack architecture allows us to do is to really drive down that cost of token economics. One of the things that Jensen talked about in the keynote but maybe not everyone heard: he talked about dynamic power and MaxQ. Maybe those are a lot of new concepts. The simple way to think about that in a data center today is that almost every data center—and we've been in the IT business for a long time—provisions power based on what the server said it took and then adds a little factor to that. But in a giga-scale data factory, you're wasting so much power. On average, in today's data center, if you want a gigawatt of provisioned power, you're probably only using 600 megawatts; you're only using 60% of the power. So one of the things we talked about, which showed up as a beautiful simulation on screen but is an actual physical thing, is the DGX data center design. We are going to be able with Vera-Rubin, and all those new racks with hardware and software controls built in, for a customer to set a power limit. We're going to stay within and hit that power limit, allowing you to use every single watt you pay for to generate tokens. Because when you go in a co-location environment or a data center environment and you ask for 100 megawatts, whether you use it or not, you pay for it. With this new technology and these new rack architectures, we're making it so much more efficient but also bringing AI into that infrastructure. It's not humans and phone calls to turn knobs; it's agents turning those knobs, making it 100% utilized in that data center but doing it safely. That's why we're operating our own gigawatt data center to prove to the world it works and to show our customers how to build that design. So you're saying the grid is overprovisioned and you're able to now provide an SLA and that's going to be giving much better power utilization. Yeah, I mean that's classic electrical grid; you always have safety margins. You talk to any data center provider and you tell them you want to run 100% and they're like, "Ugh, scary." But that's what we've got to change because we've got to take wasted power out. Everything is power limited; my house is power limited, and the data center's power limited. Since my data center is power limited and I'm paying all that cost, I want to use every watt that I can. It's only possible with the Vera-Rubin architecture because we put controls all the way down to the chip level that can react faster than humans possibly can. You know, data-in-the-loop processing automatically all the way up to AI, all the way up to human-in-the-loop things. It takes care of all of that for you. And that also drives—think about it—I can now get, at the same power payment, 40% more GPUs in a data center. How many more tokens can I get out? How much more does that lower my token economics? It's just fantastic. One of the slides Jensen showed, which he said in our private meeting was the most important slide, and he kind of joked and said, "When the audience gets quiet, that's when I'm telling you my secrets and they want to hear, 'Tell me about the next GPU.'" That's the great technology that we got from Mellanox all working together because at that very end of the scale where I need tremendous throughput and extremely low latency, that is a more expensive operation to run. But the value that you get out of that—whether that's creating new software to make a quarter-end for your customers, whether it's solving your CEO's problem, or whether it's making sure everything absolutely ships—is huge. There's some tasks that we do where you're like, "I don't care how much it costs, I just want to get it done." And that notion, now that in the same AI factory you can have both. I can serve people at a free tier and I can go all the way to the other end of the tier with the Vera-Rubin plus the LPX rack to solve the hardest coding problems in real-time. That opens up new business opportunities that were never there before. I think that's one of the big messages at GTC: it's not only new technology and not only about token economics. There's new business things that you could only think of before that weren't possible, that now with this multi-rack architecture and now with LPX at the far end of that, I can do things that I could only dream of that are now economical to do. One of the things that we talk about at theCUBE and theCUBE Research is at the macro level. If you look at the percentage of organizations' revenue that is spent on technology, it's about 4%. And we've made the prediction that that's going to double or even triple over the next 10 years as you basically scale with less labor. You're going to be tapping tokens and intelligence through tokens through APIs, and you're going to happily spend more money on that. A lot of the things that you guys are talking about really kind of resonate with us in that regard. And that's the thing Jensen talked about. I can't remember which forum he talked about it in, but everyone used to talk about Capex and as a CEO, as a CFO, or CIO, that was a bad thing. But now it's a good thing. I know if I'm spending more money and I'm getting great utilization out of it, every dollar that I spend, my revenue goes up. It used to be every dollar I spent on Capex was like, "Oh, I've got to do a refresh, it's classic IT." It's no longer that; we've broken that model. And so people want to spend more, they want to hire more agents, and they want to get more tokens because they know when they do that, their business is going to change. Their customer experience is going to change and they're going to grow more customers. Of course you want to invest. You know, I'm only limited by space, power, and availability. When you break down those things, once you have that, every dollar that I invest, I'm getting way more than a dollar back. So of course you want your Capex to grow and your infrastructure investments to go up. I see no end in sight on that one. Are you monetizing with throughput, are you monetizing with customer experience and responsiveness, or both? Because that is going to be the future of your revenue and you're going to happily spend to access that intelligence; that is your future business model. Yeah, I mean and even look at software. "About six months, it's in the next release." But if you could say, "Well, if you write me a check for this much money, you can have it tomorrow because I'm using the supercharged agent to do that." Private Credit Risks Explained, Oil Surges Above $100 | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(164.0K) ·28:19 ·57.2K views · 2026-03-20
Steve Eisman (Host, The Weekly Wrap)
> "In one part of the business, private equity buys companies, and in another part of the business, private equity lends money to itself to buy those companies.
TL;DR: A burgeoning credit cycle in the $1.8 trillion private credit market, driven by retail redemption pressures and heavy exposure to a vulnerable software sector, poses a significant risk to market stability alongside sustained triple-digit oil prices. Iran War and Energy...

TL;DR: A burgeoning credit cycle in the $1.8 trillion private credit market, driven by retail redemption pressures and heavy exposure to a vulnerable software sector, poses a significant risk to market stability alongside sustained triple-digit oil prices.

Geopolitical Tensions and Macroeconomic Volatility

  • Iran War and Energy Prices: The closure of the Strait of Hormuz has pushed oil prices above $100, dragging the S&P 500 and Nasdaq down 4% and 5% respectively for the year.
      • Inflationary Pressures: Rising oil costs are spiking fertilizer prices (up 43%) and jet fuel, threatening to embed long-term inflation if the conflict persists for months.
  • Treasury Market Divergence: Contrary to the typical "flight to safety," 10-year Treasury yields have risen from 4.0% to 4.3% as investors fear inflationary expectations.
  • Government Intervention: Treasury Secretary Besson has dismissed rumors of federal intervention to lower oil prices, citing a lack of authority and the high likelihood of leaks if such plans existed.

The Looming Private Credit Cycle

  • Rapid Market Growth: Private credit has expanded from $300 billion to $1.8 trillion in a decade. Eisman notes that credit cycles usually hit the asset classes that experienced the most recent growth.
  • The Retail Liquidity Mismatch:
      • Redemption Caps: Funds like Blackstone (BCRED), Cliffwater, Morgan Stanley, and BlackRock are seeing redemption requests exceed their 5%–7% quarterly caps.
      • Creative Funding: Blackstone recently raised its cap to 7% and used internal employee funds to meet a $400 million shortfall in redemptions.
  • The Circular PE Model: Approximately 80% of direct lending funds private equity (PE) buyouts. Eisman highlights the "circular" nature of PE firms owning private credit arms that lend to their own portfolio companies.

Sector-Specific Risks: Software and AI

  • Concentration Risk: An estimated 25% of direct lending is concentrated in software companies acquired between 2018 and 2022.
  • AI Disruption: There is growing fear that AI will eviscerate the business models of incumbent software companies at much lower price points, making these loans difficult to value.
  • The Refinancing Wall: Significant portions of these software loans (11% in 2027; 20% in 2028) were originated at low rates and must be refinanced in a high-rate environment.
  • Bank Proactivity: JP Morgan has begun marking down software loans held by private credit funds and restricting new lending to the sector, while most private credit funds continue to carry these loans at par.

Institutional and Fraud Red Flags

  • Financial Engineering: Carlyle has created "Project Potomac," a collateralized fund obligation that securitizes LP stakes to bring in new investors—a move Eisman describes as "funky" due to a lack of IPO exits.
  • Emerging Fraud: Three cases of lending fraud (TriColor, First Brands, and MFS Investment Management) have surfaced recently, signaling the start of a typical credit downturn.
  • Accounting Concerns: Canadian subprime lender GoEasy saw its stock drop 60% after revealing it booked loan payments into revenue before actual collection.

Mailbag: SoFi’s Securitization and Drug Pricing

  • SoFi’s "Rapid Amortization":
      • Trigger Breach: SoFi’s SCLP 2025-1 securitization breached its cumulative net loss (CNL) trigger (2.97% vs. 2.60% limit).
      • Impact: When a trigger is breached, the lender stops receiving its share of interest (the spread) until investors are fully repaid. This threatens SoFi's ability to fund future loans via the securitization market.
  • Healthcare and Mark Cuban:
      • Lack of Transparency: Eisman argues the U.S. drug system is broken due to Pharmaceutical Benefit Managers (PBMs) who extract rebates that don't reach consumers.
      • Innovation vs. Pricing: While critics argue regulation kills innovation, Eisman suggests the problem is "perverse incentives" and a lack of price transparency rather than the cost of research itself.

Notable Quotes

"In one part of the business, private equity buys companies, and in another part of the business, private equity lends money to itself to buy those companies. If that sounds somewhat circular to you, it's only because it is." — Steve Eisman
"Historically, when a credit cycle unfolds, the bad credits emerge in the asset class that experienced the most growth... Subprime mortgage loans grew the most [pre-2008] and blew up the most. That is why investors worry about private credit today." — Steve Eisman
"Most of the software businesses that were bought from 2018 to 2022 are lower quality than the larger companies and were trading at a much higher valuation... I am concerned about many of those taken-private companies." — John Zito (quoted by Eisman)
Hi, this is Steve Eisman, and this is another edition of the Weekly Wrap. This is for the week ending March 20th, but recorded Thursday night, March 19th. Like last week, the war in Iran dwarfs all investment considerations. As of the end of this week, the Straits of Hormuz remain closed. The Trump team is promising to open up the straits soon as China is back-channeling to allow passage for Chinese-flagged ships, and other countries are aligning transponders as Chinese adjacent, hoping for safe passage. Oil prices remain well above $100, and the S&P 500 Index is down 4% for the year now, and Nasdaq is down 5%. Here we are with a war on, and oil prices are above $100. So, of course, there are rumors that the federal government is about to intervene in oil markets to drive the price down. On Monday, Treasury Secretary Besson responded to those rumors. He said that the administration had no plans to intervene in oil markets and probably does not have the authority to do so even if it wanted to. In times of uncertainty and market volatility, conspiracy theories gain prominence. I remember that during the Great Financial Crisis, there were days when the market would rally back, and I would hear from many friends I know that they had heard that the federal government was intervening in the equity markets, and that was the reason for the rally. Now, I like a good conspiracy theory too, but the federal government intervening in equity markets never impressed me as being true. Not because it could not happen, but because if it did, it would leak, and no leaks ever occurred. Normally, in times of uncertainty, investors will seek safety and buy US Treasuries, thereby causing interest rates to go down. Not this time. Since the war began, the yield on the 10-year Treasury has climbed from 4.0% to 4.3%. Not terrible, but still in the wrong direction. Investors have been selling stocks and Treasuries. Now, why have interest rates gone up? It's all about oil prices climbing above $100. There is now a fear that if the war lasts a long time, inflation and inflationary expectations will climb. Oil has an outsized impact on prices. It affects the price of plastics. It also impacts the price of food via the cost of fertilizer. The Green Markets North American fertilizer price index has climbed from $700 per short ton before the war to $1,000, a 43% increase. Also, to offset the increased cost of jet fuel, airlines will be raising prices soon, which would impact summer travel. Bottom line, if the war ends quickly, the long-term impact on inflation will be negligible. If, however, the war lasts months, the US will develop inflationary pressures. In uncertain times like these, it's a good time to review major investment themes. So leaving aside the war, in my view, the major risks in the market are: one, will AI investments generate sufficient returns to justify the hundreds of billions being spent on AI infrastructure? And two, is a credit cycle emerging? Will it impact private credit, and if it does impact private credit, what type of loans will display problems? We've discussed the issues involving AI many times before and will continue to do so. Briefly, the major long-term issue is whether all this AI infrastructure spending will produce returns that justify the hundreds of billions being spent. Critics like Gary Marcus, who we interviewed in January, argue that it does not. However, those criticisms are not stopping AI spending. This week at NVIDIA's annual developer conference on Monday, CEO Jensen Huang said he expects purchase orders between the Blackwell and Vera Rubin chips to reach $1 trillion through 2027. So despite all the criticisms, AI spending continues at a torrid pace. And I know there are critics out there who believe that AI spending will just stop, just like internet infrastructure spending stopped during the recession of 2001. Yet, at this point, there are just no signs of AI spending slowing down. Now, let's take some time to review the private credit issues and what has happened so far this year. What is private credit and why is it so top of mind suddenly being discussed constantly on CNBC? First off, private credit operates largely in private, hence the name. Information flow is almost non-existent until problems develop, and this is why it has not been the focus of attention until now. Problems now are developing. Traditionally, private credit has been a business-to-business model. In other words, large institutions are lending money and taking measured risks to fund business development. This has been a very lucrative model for business lenders, and where there is money to be made, there is money flowing in. Like private equity firms, private credit firms are non-bank lenders that provide debt financing to companies. The terms "private credit" and "direct lending" are often used interchangeably. However, direct lending is just one subsector of the broader private credit market. Private credit has grown exponentially to a $1.8 trillion market. Private credit was only $300 billion 10 years ago and is now $1.8 trillion, so the rate of growth is huge. So why the alarm bells? Mostly because some private credit lenders began to raise funds to support their lending by appealing to retail investors. To raise money from retail investors, private credit provides some quarterly liquidity. And now for specifics. So what are the private credit strategies? First, direct lending, which is primarily an alternative to the broadly syndicated bank loan market. Lending is done directly to companies. Companies can be either sponsored, meaning backed by private equity, or non-sponsored. 80% of US direct lending goes to companies backed by private equity. In other words, direct lending funds transactions where private equity buys companies. Many private equity companies own private credit companies, and we'll talk more about that in a sec. Asset-backed finance: lending is secured by contractual cash flows and related assets. This can be as diverse as mortgages, to auto loans, to leasing, and to receivables. And lastly, opportunistic distressed credit: lending to companies in distress or other corporate actions such as spinoffs, or companies that are served neither by the direct lending nor private equity markets. Who are the lenders? The lenders are private credit funds, and most private credit funds are owned and operated by business development corporations, BDCs. A BDC is an entity whose business is to make loans. Some BDCs are public companies and some are private. And many private credit funds are operated by BDCs owned by private equity companies. So for example, the largest private credit lender is the Blackstone Private Credit Fund, otherwise known as BCRED, which is a non-public BDC wholly owned by Blackstone, the private equity company, which itself is public. So in one part of the business, private equity buys companies, and in another part of the business, private equity lends money to itself to buy those companies. If that sounds somewhat circular to you, it's only because it is. Now, private credit has two major issues: should private credit funds have been sold to retail investors, and is a credit cycle emerging? The two issues are interrelated, but they are not the same. A credit cycle means that losses on loans are increasing. Now, there are bad credit cycles, which would be the Great Financial Crisis, and not-so-bad credit cycles, which would be the recession of 2001. The US has not had any kind of credit cycle at all since the Great Financial Crisis, which is why many commentators think we are on the cusp of one. We are overdue. And before we get to the actual issues, let me make an important caveat. Since the Great Financial Crisis, there has been a kind of competition to see who could predict the next calamity. Every time an issue surfaces, someone seizes on it and argues that this is it. This is the issue that will cause a recession or another crisis or calamity. And then nothing happens because the US economy is incredibly resilient. Private credit is the issue du jour. I take the issues involving private credit very seriously, but the problems of private credit are still early. This is an emerging story and it is still too early to tell how bad it will get. Since these private credit loans are not publicly traded, they are, by definition, illiquid. And there is nothing wrong with that. As I explained a few minutes ago, historically the investors in private credit funds were large sophisticated institutions. They know what they are getting into: a higher yielding product that is illiquid. Once private credit funds finished tapping the entire institutional world, they decided to seek new investors in the retail world: brokerage clients, 401(k) plans, etc. And here's where I have a problem. The loans are illiquid. So to create the appearance of liquidity for retail investors, redemptions are allowed, but they are capped depending on the fund at 5% to 7% of assets per quarter. I am sure that all of this is disclosed to retail investors. I am sure that many retail investors did not understand the full implications. Now that redemption levels consistently breach the 5% to 7% threshold, private credit funds have had to get creative or just limit redemptions. One more important detail: private credit is overexposed to software companies. Software company valuations have declined en masse due to AI's impact on the traditional business model of software companies. With respect to the credit cycle question, it bears repeating that 80% of direct lending loans are for buyouts of companies by private equity. And that is why the direct lending industry is overexposed to software companies. Some commentators estimate that 25% of direct lending loans are to software companies, the companies private equity bought from 2018 through 2022. The fear, and it is a fear that is hotly debated, is that AI is going to make massive inroads into the software sector at a much cheaper price point and eviscerate the business models of the software incumbents. Another problem is that it is estimated that 11% of these software loans need to be refinanced in 2027 and another 20% in 2028. Now, those loans have very low interest rates because they were originated when rates were considerably lower. They will be refinanced, if they are refinanced, at rates that are considerably higher. Assuming the turmoil in the software sector continues, who is going to refinance these loans? It's a very important question. Now, everyone is focused on private credit because that is where the loan growth has been. Historically, when a credit cycle unfolds, the bad credits emerge in the asset class that experienced the most growth. Exactly that occurred during the Great Financial Crisis. Subprime mortgage loans grew the most and blew up the most. That is why investors worry about private credit today. It's where the growth has been. Since the GFC, the banks have not generated much loan growth at all. Almost all of the loan growth that has occurred is in private credit, which was only $300 billion 10 years ago and is now $1.8 trillion. Hey, it's Steve Eisman. That's shopify.com/eisman. Now let's review some of the more recent private credit events as well as other related news. By the way, sometimes it seems like every day there is new news on private credit, and that news is always bad. A differential diagnosis is needed. Are we really in the beginning of a major credit cycle, or are we in the middle of a major news cycle trying to predict the next devastating, market-destroying crisis? Too early to tell. So here are the events, the most recent ones. One: Blackstone's large $82 billion private credit fund called BCRED received a redemption notice, a quarterly redemption notice of 7.9% of assets. The fund redemption cap is 5%. However, for this quarter, Blackstone raised the cap to 7% and funded the remaining 400 million from Blackstone itself plus employees ponied up some money. Two: Cliffwater. Investors put in a 14% redemption notice from the $33 billion Cliffwater Corporate Lending Fund. The fund only honored its 7% cap amount, leaving half of the redemptions unfulfilled. Three: Morgan Stanley. Investors put in a 10.9% redemption notice from the 8 billion Morgan Stanley North Haven Private Income Fund. Morgan Stanley honored only the 5% cap amount. Four: BlackRock. Investors put in a 9.3% redemption notice for the 26 billion BlackRock H-Land Fund. Redemptions were honored only at the 5% cap. Five: JP Morgan. This might be the most important news of all. As I said, private credit funds are overexposed to software, with some estimating 25% of all direct lending loans in that asset class alone. JP Morgan announced that it had marked down loans held by private credit funds, specifically software company loans. JP Morgan also began restricting its own lending to software companies. Now, while the loans, I assume, are still performing, JP Morgan is being proactive. So at JP Morgan, these loans are no longer valued at par. And I would bet, however, that the exact same loans are still valued at par at most private credit funds. Six: Carlyle. This is not a private credit issue, but it is adjacent. Private equity is having problems returning capital to investors because of a lack of IPOs. Carlyle got creative. It structured a collateralized fund obligation called Project Potomac that pools limited partnership stakes from multiple Carlyle private equity funds into one securitization. The securitization is sliced and diced into debt, preferred and common equity, and sold to investors. This way, Carlyle brings in new investors to buy out old investors. Sounds funky. Only because it is. Seven: MFS. Prior to February of this year, only two lending frauds emerged, TriColor and First Brands. MFS Investment Management is now the third fraud, and the CEO is accused of siphoning funds. Eight: GoEasy. Now, this is not a private credit issue either, but it sure is a credit quality issue. Canada's GoEasy is a subprime lender that makes personal, auto, and home equity loans. The company booked loan payments into revenue before collections. I'll say that again. The company booked loan payments into revenue before collections. A no-no. And that is a premature recognition of revenue. And upon announcing it, the stock fell 60% last week. Nine: Executives at the biggest private credit funds have also sought to play down the redemption story and the unfolding credit cycle issue. However, John Zito, co-president of Apollo's asset management arm, spoke more bluntly about these issues in a discussion that UBS arranged for some of its clients. Zito was speaking off the record, but somehow his words were reported in the Wall Street Journal. With respect to private credit's overexposure to software, Zito said, quote, "Most of the software businesses that were bought from 2018 to 2022 are lower quality than the larger companies and were trading at a much higher valuation. And so I am concerned about many of those taken private companies," unquote. It's clear from his words that he expects loan losses in software. I'd point out that Apollo only has a 2% exposure to software as opposed to the industry's 25%. So Zito can comfortably be critical of his competitors. A nice position to be in. He also expects elevated redemptions for several more quarters. And he also pointed out the contradiction in that there is still large demand for buying secondary private equity positions, which is buying out the position of a limited partner in a private equity fund, but those same investors who are doing that, who are bullish on private equity, are nervous about private credit, which finances 80% of private equity. It's a very good point. There is no doubt in my mind that a credit cycle is emerging. How bad it will be and who will experience losses is as yet unknown. But we will be exploring these issues in future podcasts, I am sure. I received a related question from a viewer. The question is from Adam, who asks me to elaborate on the securitization issues I mentioned briefly in a prior episode. SoFi is a public online personal finance company with a market cap of about 22 billion. It makes various kinds of consumer loans. To answer Adam's question requires a bit of a securitization tutorial. So here goes. Lending is a low-margin business. So low, in fact, that returns are abysmal if all you do is lend out your own money. To generate a sufficient return, all lending requires leverage. Now, banks use deposits to create that leverage. You put your money in the bank. The bank pays you nothing on your checking account and something on savings and CDs. The bank then takes your money and lends it out at a higher interest rate than what they are paying you. That spread is called the bank's net interest margin. If you are not a bank, then you have to find leverage elsewhere. And one place to find it is via securitization. A lender will make let's say 1 billion in consumer loans. The lender will pool those loans into a securitization and sell the securitization, which is a debt instrument, to investors. The interest rate that the lender charges the consumer will be higher than the interest rate it pays to the securitization investor. You ask why? Because the risk of making a loan to one person is higher than the risk of a pool of loans to thousands of people. The difference between what the lender charges the borrowers and what it pays to the securitization investors is the spread or net interest margin for the lender. Let's say for example, the lender charges borrowers 10% and the securitization investors receive a yield of 5%. Every month, the lender receives 10% from the borrowers, gives 5% to the securitization investors, and keeps 5% for itself. Here's the catch. Securitization investors want protection against losses above a certain amount. When losses breach a certain level, called the cumulative net loss trigger, the securitization goes into what's called rapid amortization. Now, what does that mean? In the case of SoFi, January losses for SoFi's SCLP 2025-1 securitization appear to have breached the deal's cumulative net loss trigger, with Bloomberg reporting CNLs of 2.97% versus the trigger level of only 2.60%. When a securitization breaches its CNL trigger, the lender, in this case SoFi, no longer receives any payments. Remember that in our hypothetical every month the lender receives 10% from the borrowers, gives 5% to the securitization investors, and keeps 5% for itself. Now that SoFi's deal has breached the CNL trigger, SoFi is no longer getting its 5%. The entire 10% goes to the securitization investors until they are paid back everything. Interestingly, it looks like the SoFi 2025-2 securitization will soon breach its trigger as well. This is potentially a disaster for SoFi. SoFi needs the securitization market to fund its loans. If securitization investors decide they no longer want SoFi paper, SoFi will not be able to lend. Our second question comes from Michael, who has a question related to our recent interview with Mark Cuban. Michael says, quote, "Mr. Eisman, Mark Cuban is entertaining and his Cost Plus Drugs serves a useful purpose at the margins. However, your discussion with him failed to answer the crux of the matter: are there parts of the system that are unjustly extracting rents that cause drug prices to be high without corresponding benefits? I submit that while parts of the system could be improved, the drug development ecosystem is doing what people want: generating innovative new medicines. If we regulate drug prices, there will be less innovation. You probably don't realize it, but it is a fact that here in the US we pay less for generic drugs, the mainstay of Cost Plus, than in any other developed country that have regulated drug pricing. Generic drugs make up 90% of prescriptions, and the place where our costs are much higher is in proprietary branded drugs making up 10% of prescriptions. The high cost of these in the US supports research and development that ultimately benefits patients everywhere in the world. If drug prices would be regulated in the US, capital would not flow to drug innovation. We need to decide as a country how much innovation we want. We have not had that discussion," end quote. This is a very fair question, so let me address it. I don't think Mark Cuban has any interest in regulating drug pricing. That is certainly not my impression. His argument, which I do find compelling, is that the drug delivery system in the United States is horribly non-transparent and has perverse incentives that add significantly to drug pricing. For example, the US is the only country in the world with PBMs, pharmaceutical benefit managers. PBMs negotiate rebates from pharma manufacturers. The wholesale price of a drug might be $1,000, but the PBM negotiates a rebate of let's say $500. According to Mark Cuban, that rebate does not go to the consumer but to the PBM and the employer. Before the rebate, the pharma company got $1,000. Now it's only getting $500, so it now has a built-in incentive to raise drug prices to try and offset the rebate. This is just one example, but I think it shows that a great deal of the problem with high drug prices has less to do with the cost of creating drugs and more to do with the perverse incentives embedded in our drug delivery system. Our entire drug pricing system lacks transparency. People are charged different prices for the same drug depending upon their insurance plans. Cost Plus provides one price to all. It is the wholesale price plus 15% for everybody. The hope is that providing such transparency over time will push drug prices down. This last Monday, we did post an interview with Mark Cuban which I just referenced. Mark runs a private company called Cost Plus that delivers pharmaceuticals in the United States at a price of wholesale plus a 15% markup. Mark is trying to reform our pharma system with transparency and lower prices. Our discussion focused on our broken pharma pricing system as well as issues in the overall healthcare system. I learned a lot during the interview, and I think you will too, so check it out. This Monday we are posting an interview with Michael Ha, the managed care analyst at Baird. Michael is a recurring guest. We discuss many of the issues Mark Cuban brought up with respect to the pricing of pharmaceuticals, but we also discuss why the entire managed care industry is doing so poorly in every one of its businesses.
