Terence Tao – How the world’s top mathematician uses AI

Dwarkesh Patel(1.2M) ·83:44 ·111.5K views · 2026-03-20
Terence Tao (Mathematician) Dwarkesh Patel (Host, Dwarkesh Podcast)
> "AI has basically driven the cost of idea generation down to almost zero...
TL;DR: While AI has reduced the cost of hypothesis generation to near zero, the bottleneck in scientific discovery has shifted to verification and "depth." Currently, AI functions as a "breadth" tool—capable of exhaustively applying known techniques—while humans remain essential...

TL;DR: While AI has reduced the cost of hypothesis generation to near zero, the bottleneck in scientific discovery has shifted to verification and "depth." Currently, AI functions as a "breadth" tool—capable of exhaustively applying known techniques—while humans remain essential for the cumulative, adaptive reasoning required to solve "mile-high" frontier problems.

The Shift from Theory to Data-Driven Discovery

  • Historical Context: The discovery of planetary motion transitioned from "beautiful" but incorrect geometric theories (Kepler’s Platonic solids) to data-driven empirical laws. It took Newton a century to provide the theoretical "why" for Kepler's "what."
  • The Modern Paradigm: Science has moved through four stages: Theory, Experiment, Numerical Simulation, and now Big Data.
      • Reverse Scientific Method: Traditionally, scientists formed a hypothesis and tested it against data. Today, we increasingly collect massive datasets first and use machine learning to extract laws and patterns (e.g., modern "data science" math).
  • The Bottleneck Shift: Idea generation is no longer the constraint; the "internet-ification" of ideas means we are now overwhelmed by "AI slop." The new bottleneck is Verification and Evaluation—determining which of a thousand generated theories actually moves a subject forward.

Current Performance: AI in Mathematics

  • The Erdos Problems: Over the last few months, AI assistants solved roughly 50 out of 1,100+ Erdos problems.
      • Plateau Reached: Progress has stalled because the "low-hanging fruit" (problems solvable by one-shot AI jumps) has been picked.
      • Success Rates: While AI "wins" are publicized, systematic studies show a 1–2% success rate on any given hard problem. Success usually comes from AI being applied at massive scale, not through superior reasoning.
  • Artificial Cleverness vs. Intelligence: Tao distinguishes between "jumping machines" and true intelligence.
      • Cleverness: The ability to "jump" and solve a problem through brute-force trial and error or applying obscure existing techniques.
      • Intelligence: A cumulative, adaptive process where two collaborators build a prototype strategy, test it, fail, and stay at a "handhold" to pull the idea further. AI currently lacks this cumulative "partial progress" capability.

Human-AI Complementarity and Productivity

  • Breadth vs. Depth: AI excels at Breadth (checking every standard technique across a million problems simultaneously). Humans excel at Depth (focusing on one "mile-high" cliff for decades).
      • Redesigning Science: Science is currently structured for depth because that is the limit of human expertise. Future science will be "unrecognizable" once we build paradigms that leverage AI’s ability to map out entire fields and identify "islands of difficulty" for humans.
  • Productivity Gains: Tao reports being 2x to 5x more productive on auxiliary tasks.
      • Secondary Tasks: AI handles reformatting, literature searches, generating complex plots, and writing boilerplate code for simulations.
      • The Core remains Human: For the actual "Eureka" moments and solving the most resistant 20% of a problem, Tao still relies on pen and paper.

The Risks of Optimization and "Lost Serendipity"

  • The Loss of Randomness: Modern tools (and remote work) optimize for efficiency but destroy serendipity.
      • The Library Analogy: In the past, searching for one journal article led to accidentally discovering another interesting one nearby. Now, search engines and AI provide exactly what is requested, removing the "high-temperature" randomness necessary for inspiration.
  • The Hedgehog and the Fox: Tao identifies as a "Fox" (knowing a little about everything) and warns that over-optimizing a career can lead to boredom and a lack of new ideas.
  • The Future of the Field: Mathematics will not disappear, but it will scale. Just as calculators ended the era of humans manually computing log tables, AI will automate "handcrafted" proofs, allowing mathematicians to study ecosystems of problems rather than individual equations.
"AI has basically driven the cost of idea generation down to almost zero... Now the bottleneck is different. We're in a situation where people can generate thousands of theories... and now we have to verify them, evaluate them." — Terence Tao
"A lot of what a 19th-century mathematician would do, you could make a call to Mathematica or an AI and it would just solve the problem in a few minutes. But we moved on... Genetics is not dead as a subject just because you can sequence a genome for a thousand dollars. You move to a different scale." — Terence Tao
Okay today I'm chatting with Terence Tao, who needs no introduction. Terence, I want to begin by having you retell the story of how Kepler discovered the laws of planetary motion, because I think this will be a great jumping off point to talk about AI for math. Okay, yeah. So I've always had an amateur interest in astronomy, and so I've loved stories of how the early astronomers worked out the nature of the universe. Kepler was building on the work of Copernicus, who was in himself building on the work of Aristarchus. Copernicus very famously proposed the heliocentric model—that instead of the planets and the sun going around the Earth, the sun was at the center of the solar system and the other planets were going around the sun. Copernicus proposed that the orbits of the planets were perfect circles. His theory kind of fit the observations that the Greeks and the Arabs and the Indians had worked out over centuries. I think Kepler got interested as he learned about these theories in his studies, and he made this observation that the ratios of the size of the orbits that Copernicus predicted seemed to have some geometric meaning. I think he started proposing that you know, if you take say the orbit of the Earth and you enclose it in I think maybe a cube, the outer sphere that encloses the cube almost matched perfectly the orbit of Mars and so forth. There were six planets known at the time, five gaps between them, and there were five perfect Platonic solids: the cube, the tetrahedron, icosahedron, octahedron and dodecahedron. He had this theory which he thought was absolutely beautiful—that you could inscribe these Platonic solids between the spheres of the planets—and it seemed to fit. It seemed to him like God's design of the planets was matching this mathematical perfection of the Platonic solids. So he needed data to confirm this theory. At the time there was only one really high quality data set in existence, which was Tycho Brahe's. This Danish astronomer, a very wealthy eccentric astronomer, had managed to convince the Danish government to fund this extremely expensive observatory. It was in fact an entire island where he had taken decades of observations of all the planets—Mars, Jupiter—every night, or at least every night for which the weather was clear, with the naked eye. He was the last of the naked eye astronomers, and so he had all this data which Kepler could use to confirm his theory. Kepler started working with Tycho, but Tycho was very jealous of the data. He only gave him a little bit—bit bit bits of it at a time. I think Kepler eventually just stole the data, actually. He co- he copied it and had to have a fight with Brahe's descendants. He did get the data, and then he worked out to kind of his disappointment that his beautiful theory didn't quite work. Like the data was sort of off from his Platonic solid theory by about 10% or something. He tried all kinds of fudges, moving the circles around and things, and it didn't quite work. But he worked on this problem for years and years and eventually he figured out how to use the data to work out the actual orbits of the planets. That was an incredibly clever, genius amount of data analysis. Then he eventually worked out that the orbits were actually ellipses, not circles, which was shocking for him. Then he worked out the two laws of planetary motion: the ellipses and also that equal areas sweep out equal times. Then ten years later, after collecting a lot of data, the furthest planets like Saturn and Jupiter—which were the hardest for him to work out—he finally worked out this third law also. That the time it takes for a planet to complete its orbit was proportional to some power of the distance to the sun. These are the three famous Kepler's laws of motion. He had no explanation for them. It was just all driven by experiment, and it took Newton a century later to give a theory that explained all three laws at once. The take I want to try on you is that Kepler was a high-temperature LLM, where Newton comes up with this explanation of why the three laws of planetary motion must be true. The way that Kepler discovers the laws of planetary motion or figures out the relative orbits of the different planets is, as you say, a work of genius, but then through his career he's just trying random relationships. In fact, in the book in which he writes down the third law of planetary motion, it's sort of an aside on the harmonics of the world, which is this book about how all these different planets have these different harmonies. The reason there's so much famine and misery on Earth is because the Earth is mi-fa-mi; that's the note of Earth. And so all this random astrology, but in there is the cube-square law, which tells you what relationship the period has to a planet's distance from the sun. As you were detailing, if you add that to Newton's F=ma and then the equation for centripetal acceleration, you get the inverse square law and so Newton works that out. But the reason I think this is an interesting story is I feel like LLMs could do the kind of thing of: "20 years, let's try random relationships, some of which make no sense." As long as there's a verifiable data bank like Brahe's data set where okay, I'm gonna try out random things about musical notes, I'm gonna try out random things about Platonic objects, all these different geometries. I have this bias that there's some important thing about the geometry of these orbits and then one thing works. As long as you can verify it, it can then drive these empirical regularities, which can then drive actual deep scientific progress. Traditionally, when we talk about the history of science, idea generation has always been kind of the prestige part of science. A scientific problem comes with many steps. You have to identify a problem, then you have to identify a good problem to work on—a fruitful problem. Then you need to collect data, you need to figure out a strategy to analyze the data, to make a hypothesis. At this point you need to propose a good hypothesis, and then you need to validate, and then you need to write things up and explain. There's a dozen different components. But yeah, the ones we celebrate are these Eureka genius moments of idea generation. Kepler certainly had to, as you say, cycle through many ideas, and several which didn't work and I bet many that he didn't even publish at all because they just didn't fit. And that's an important part of the process: trying all kinds of random things and seeing if they worked. But as you say, they have to be matched by an equal amount of verification otherwise it's slop. I mean, we celebrate Kepler, but we should also celebrate Brahe for his assiduous data collection, which was 10 times more precise than any previous observation. That extra decimal point of accuracy was actually essential for Kepler to get his results. And he was using Euclidean geometry and the most advanced mathematics he could use at the time to match his models with the data. So all aspects had to be in play: the data and the theory and the hypothesis generation. I'm not sure nowadays that hypothesis generation is the bottleneck anymore. Science has changed in the century since. Classically, the two big paradigms for science were theory and experiment. Then in the 20th century, numerical simulation came along, and so you can also do computer simulations to test theories. But then finally in the late 20th century we had big data, the era of data analysis. A lot of new progress is actually driven now by analyzing massive data sets—first collecting large data sets and then drawing the patterns from them to deduce laws—which is a little bit different from how science used to work where you make a few observations or you just have one out of the blue idea and then you collect data to test your idea. That's a classic scientific method. Now it's almost reverse; you collect big data first and then you try to get hypotheses from it. Kepler was maybe one of the first early data scientists, but even he didn't start with Tycho's data set and analyze it. He had some preconceived theories first. But it seems that this is less and less the way we make progress, just because the data is so much more massive; it's just so much more useful. Oh interesting, I actually feel like the modern 20th century science that you're describing actually very well describes what happened with Kepler, where he did have these ideas. 1595 and 96 is where he comes up with first polygons and then the Platonic objects theory, but they were wrong. And then a few years later he gets Brahe's data, and it's only after 20 years of just trying random things that he gets this empirical regularity. And so it actually feels closer to Brahe's data being analogous to some massive data bank of simulations and then, now that you've got the data, you can keep trying random things. But if it wasn't for the data, Kepler would be out there just writing books about harmonics and Platonic objects and there would be nothing to actually verify against. Yeah, yeah, yeah, so the data was extremely important. But the distinction I was trying to make was that sort of traditionally you make a hypothesis and then you test it against data. But now with machine learning and data analysis and statistics, you can start with data and through say statistics work out laws that were not present before. Kepler's third law is a little bit like this, except that for the third law, instead of having the thousand data points that Brahe had, Kepler had like six data points. Every planet he knew the length of the orbit and the distance to the sun, and there were like five or six data points and he did what we would now call regression. If he could fit a curve to these six data points and he got a square-cube law, which was amazing, but actually he was quite lucky that these six data points gave him the right conclusion. That's not enough data to be really reliable. There was a later astronomer, Johann Bode, who took the same data, actually the distances to the planets, and inspired by Kepler he had a prediction that the distances to the planets formed basically a shifted geometric progression. He also fit a curve, except that there was one point missing, so there was a big gap between Mars and Jupiter. His law predicted that there was a missing planet. It was a kind of a crank theory, except when Uranus was discovered by Herschel, the distance Uranus fit exactly this pattern. And then Ceres was discovered, this asteroid in the asteroid belt, and it also fit the pattern. People got really excited that Bode had discovered this amazing new law of nature. But then Neptune was discovered and it was completely way off. Basically it was just a numerical fluke—there were six data points. So maybe one reason why Kepler didn't highlight his third law as much as the first two laws is that maybe instinctively, even though he didn't have modern statistics, he kind of knew that with six data points you have to be somewhat tentative with the conclusions. It sounds like you don't think the bottleneck in science is finding more things that are, for each given field, their equivalent of the third law of planetary motion so that then later on somebody can say, "Oh we need a way to explain this, let's work out the math, here is the inverse square law of gravity." Right, so I think AI has basically driven the cost of idea generation down to almost zero, in a very similar way to how the internet drove the cost of communication down to almost zero. Which is an amazing thing, but it doesn't create abundance by itself. Now the bottleneck is different. We're now in a situation where suddenly people can generate thousands of theories for a given scientific problem and now we have to verify them and evaluate them. So I think there's this incredibly interesting question: if you have billions of AI scientists, not only how do you gauge which ones are real progress, but how do you—I mean, this is actually a question that human science has had to face and we've solved somehow. Right, so a lot of it is the test of time. Many great ideas didn't actually get a great reception at the time that they were first proposed. Yeah, it seems often in the history of science when a new theory comes up that in retrospect we realize is correct, it seems to make implications that just either make no sense because they're wrong and we realize later on why they're wrong, or they're correct but seem wildly implausible at the time. As you've talked about, Aristarchus had heliocentrism in the third century BC, and then the ancient Athenians were like, "This can't be because if the Earth is going around the sun, we should see the relative position of the stars change as we're going around the sun." The only way that wouldn't be the case is if they're so far away that you don't notice any parallax, which is actually the correct implication. But there's times when actually the implication isn't correct and we just need to graduate to a better level of understanding. Leibniz would chide Newton and disagree with Newton's theory of gravity on the basis that it implied action at a distance and then there's—we don't know the mechanism, and Newton himself was sort of stunned that inertial mass and gravitational mass were the same quantity. All these things later were resolved by Einstein. But it was still progress. And so the question for a system of peer review for AI would be even if you can falsify a theory, how would you notice that it still constitutes progress relative to the thing before? Yeah, so it often actually, the ultimately correct theory initially is worse in many ways. Copernicus's theory of the planets—it was less accurate than Ptolemy's theory. Geocentrism had been developed for a millennium by that point, and they had made many, many tweaks and increasingly complicated ad hoc fixes to make it more and more accurate. Copernicus's theory was a lot simpler but much less accurate. It was only Kepler that made it more accurate than Ptolemy's theory. Science is always a work in progress, so you—when you only get part of the solution, it looks worse than a theory which is incorrect but somehow has been completed to the point where it kind of answers all the questions. As you say, Newton's theory had big mysteries, like the equivalence of mass and action at a distance, which were only resolved with a very conceptually different approach centuries afterwards. Often progress has to be made actually not by adding more theories, but by deleting some assumptions that you have in your mind. One reason why geocentrism held on for so long is we had this idea that objects naturally want to stay at rest—this is the Aristotelian notion of physics. And so the idea that the Earth was moving—you know, how come we weren't all sort of falling over? Once you have Newton's laws of motion—an object in motion remains in motion and so forth—then it makes sense. But you had to sort of conceptually—it's a very big conceptual leap to realize that the Earth is in motion. It doesn't feel like it's in motion. And like the biggest advances—Darwin's theory of evolution—is the idea that species are not static, but it's something which is not obvious because you don't see evolution in your lifetime. Well, now we can actually, but it seems permanent and static. Right now we're going through a cognitive version of the Copernican revolution, where we used to think that human intelligence is the center of the universe and now we're actually seeing that there's very different types of intelligence that are out there with very different strengths and weaknesses. And so our assessment of which tasks require intelligence and which ones don't has to be reordered quite a bit. And so, yeah, trying to fit AI into our theories of scientific progress and what is hard and what is easy, we're struggling quite a bit. We have to ask questions that we've never really had to ask before. Or maybe the philosophers had, but now we all have to deal with it. Okay, speaking of clever ideas, one of my listeners, Shawn, solved the puzzle that Jane Street made for my audience and posted a great walkthrough on X. For context, Jane Street trained a ResNet and then shuffled all 96 layers and then challenged people to put them back in the right order using only the model's outputs and training data. You can't brute-force this. There's more possible orderings than atoms in the universe. So Shawn broke the problem into two different parts. 1. Pair the layers into 48 blocks. And 2. Put the blocks into the correct order. For pairing, Shawn realized that in a well-trained ResNet, the product of two weight matrices in a residual block should have a distinctive negative diagonal pattern. And this arises as a way for the model to keep the residual stream from growing out of control. From this insight, he was able to recover the right pairings. For ordering, Shawn noticed that the model seemed to improve if he sorted the blocks by the size of their residual contributions. Starting with that rough approximation, he combined a clever ranking heuristic with local swaps to recover the exact right order. His full walkthrough is linked in the description. Don't worry if you didn't get to this puzzle in time, though. There's still one up about backdoor LLMs that even Jane Street doesn't know how to solve. You can find it at janestreet.com/dwarkesh. It seems to be a lot more trial and error and just repetition brute force, which can—it scales and it can work amazingly well in certain contexts, but yeah, this idea of sort of building up cumulatively from from partial progress is kind of what's still not quite there yet. Interesting. You were saying like if Gemini Pro or Claude 3.5 whatever solves a problem, it is not the case that its own understanding of math has progressed, or even if it works on a problem without solving it, it's not that its own understanding of math has progressed. Maybe what you just did is part of 1 or 0.001% of the training data for the next generation, so maybe eventually some of it gets absorbed. But yeah. So Terence talks about the importance of decomposing particularly gnarly problems into a series of easier chunks. But models today tend to struggle with these kinds of problem-solving techniques. That's where Labelbox comes in. Labelbox helps you train models not just to get the right answer, but to think the right way. They've operationalized these reasoning behaviors into rubrics, giving you the ability to evaluate every important dimension of a model's output. These rubrics go beyond simple correctness. Did the model reach for the right tools? Did it check its own work and explore alternative paths? How clear was its response? These skills are useful across domains—math, physics, finance, psychology and more—and they're becoming increasingly important as models take on harder, open-ended problems, some of which have multiple solutions and some of which we don't even know the solutions to. Labelbox can get you rubrics tailored to your domain, helping you systematically measure and shape how your models think. Learn more at labelbox.com/dwarkesh. Gauss was interested in the prime numbers and he created one of the first mathematical data sets. He computed the first hundred thousand prime numbers or so, hoping to find patterns. And he did find a pattern, but maybe not the pattern he was expecting. He found a statistical pattern in the primes—that if you count how many primes there are up to 100, 1000, 1 million and so forth, they get sparser and sparser, but the drop off in the density was inversely proportional to the natural logarithm of the range of numbers. So he conjectured what we now call the prime number theorem. The number of primes up to x is like x divided by the natural log of x. He had no way to prove this; it was data driven. So this conjecture was revolutionary for its time because it was maybe the first really important conjecture of math that was statistical in nature. Normally you would talk about patterns like maybe the spacing between the primes has a certain regularity or something. But this was really something which didn't tell you exactly how many primes there were in any given range; it just gave you an approximation that got better and better as you went further and further out. It helped—it started the field of what we call analytic number theory. It was the first in many conjectures like this, many of which got proved, which sort of started consolidating the idea that the prime numbers actually didn't really have a pattern—that they behaved like random sets of numbers with a certain density. I mean, they had some patterns like, like they're almost all odd; so there's some. And they're not actually random; they're what's called pseudo-random. I mean, there's no random number generation involved in creating the prime numbers. But over time it became more and more productive to think of the primes as as if they were just generated by some God rolling dice all the time and creating this random set. This allowed us to make all these other predictions. So there's this still-open conjecture in number theory, the twin prime conjecture, that there should be infinitely many pairs of primes that are twins—distance two apart, like 11 and 13. We can't prove that, and there's actually good reasons why we can't prove it, but but because of this statistical random model of the primes, we are absolutely convinced it's true. Okay, we know that if the primes were sort of generated by flipping coins or something, then we would just by random chance—just like infinite monkeys at a typewriter—we would see twin primes appear over and over again. And we have over time developed this very accurate conceptual model of what the primes should behave like based on statistics and probability, but it's all mostly heuristic and non-rigorous, but extremely accurate. So the few times we actually can prove things about the primes, it has matched up with the predictions of this random model of the primes. So we have this conjectural conceptual framework for understanding the primes that we every everyone believes in. And you know, it's the same reason why we believe the Riemann hypothesis is true, why we believe that cryptography based on the primes is basically is mathematically secure, things like that—it's all part of this this this belief. In fact, one reason why we care about the Riemann hypothesis is that if the Riemann hypothesis failed, we knew it was false, it means that there would it would be a serious blow to this model. That this it would mean there's a secret pattern to the primes that we were not aware of. And I think we would very rapidly abandon any cryptography based on the primes because if there was one pattern that we didn't know about, there's probably more, and these patterns can lead to exploits in crypto and, yeah, it's going to be it'd be a big big shock. So we really want to make sure that that doesn't happen. So yeah, it's so we've been convinced of things like the Riemann hypothesis and things over time, but some of it is experimental evidence, some is the few times we've been able to make theoretical results, they've always aligned. You know, it is possible that the consensus is wrong and and we've all just missed something very basic. You know, there have been paradigm shifts in the past in scientific history. AWS AI and Data Conference 2026 - Keynote: The Age of Agents | AWS Events

AWS Events(173.0K) ·72:09 ·198 views · 2026-03-20
The "Age of Agents" marks a fundamental shift from generative AI experimentation to autonomous systems that reason, plan, and execute complex workflows.
TL;DR The "Age of Agents" marks a fundamental shift from generative AI experimentation to autonomous systems that reason, plan, and execute complex workflows. Success in this era requires transitioning from pilots to production-grade deployments by prioritizing a secure data...

TL;DR

The "Age of Agents" marks a fundamental shift from generative AI experimentation to autonomous systems that reason, plan, and execute complex workflows. Success in this era requires transitioning from pilots to production-grade deployments by prioritizing a secure data foundation, operational excellence, and "security as an accelerator."

The Shift to Agentic AI (2022–2026)

  • The Three-Stage Evolution: AWS defines the rapid progression of AI capabilities over the last four years:
      • 2022 (Static Answers): AI functioned as a "brilliant answer machine" without memory or context.
      • 2024 (Memory and Context): Models became "colleagues," gaining the ability to query databases and hold conversational threads.
      • 2026 (Autonomous Agents): Current systems are "thinking partners" that receive a goal, reason through the steps, and execute independently.
  • Speed to Value: Richie Jones highlighted that the competitive gap is widening between companies stuck in "pilot theater" and those removing friction between concept and customer impact.
  • The AWS Agent Stack: A tiered architecture for scaling agents:
      • Foundation: SageMaker and Compute (purpose-built silicon).
      • Managed Layer: Amazon Bedrock (frontier models and built-in guardrails).
      • Builder Tools: AgentCore and Strands (open-source framework for model-driven agents).
      • Applications: Agent-enabled tools like Amazon Quick and Amazon KIRO.

Security and Governance as Accelerators

  • "Built-in, Not Bolted-on": Hart Rossman emphasized that security must be the architecture's foundation to prevent compliance "fire drills" that kill momentum.
      • Nitro Platform: Provides confidential computing by default with zero operator access.
      • Encryption Standards: All 117 AWS services that store customer data, including agentic services, are encrypted by default.
  • Agentic IDE (Amazon KIRO): A shift toward "spec-driven development" where agents generate technical specifications and threat models from a prompt before writing code.
  • Compliance Scoping Matrices: AWS launched new matrices specifically for Generative AI and Agentic computing to help regulated industries align technology with policy.

Modernizing the Data Foundation

  • Building at the "Scale of an Idea": Tobias Ternstrom noted that agents will lead to a 1,000x increase in applications and data, requiring effortless data estate management.
  • Database Velocity: To support agentic iterations, AWS has reduced database creation times (DynamoDB and Aurora PostgreSQL Serverless) from minutes to seconds.
  • Operational Data Access:
      • Legacy Integration: High-value data in Oracle, SQL Server, and DB2 is now more accessible to agents via MCP (Model Context Protocol) servers.
      • Oracle Database @ AWS: Now live in Dublin, allowing Exadata hardware to run with low latency next to AI agents.
  • Zero-ETL and Savings: Expanded Zero-ETL support for SQL Server and Oracle to Redshift, and the introduction of a Database Savings Plan offering up to 35% discounts across database services.

Operationalizing Talent and Legacy Systems

  • AWS Transform: A new agentic service designed to compress 18-month migrations into weeks.
      • Impact: Has already saved 810,000 hours of manual effort and mapped 1.1 billion lines of mainframe COBOL/JCL code.
  • The Human Element (Workhuman): CEO Eric Mosley demonstrated how AI agents analyze "information-dense" recognition data to identify hidden influencers and high-potential leaders.
      • Predictive Analytics: AI can now predict promotions up to two years in advance by analyzing social graphs and the "extraordinary language" colleagues use to describe top performers.
  • Productivity Benchmarks:
      • Organizations using agents see a 33% structural increase in productivity.
      • By 2028, 15% of all enterprise decisions are expected to be made autonomously by agents.
"We've moved beyond experimentation, beyond pilots, beyond proof of concept theatre. We are now in the era of intelligent systems that reason, plan, act, and learn alongside us." — Fiona Simpson, AWS
"Speed is a decision... You don't have to choose between speed and security. You don't have to choose between innovation and compliance." — Hart Rossman, VP of Security, AWS
"The question is no longer 'Can AI do this?' The question is 'Are you building on the right foundation?'" — Richie Jones, Enterprise Technologist, AWS
"Who can be a builder is all of a sudden anyone. And how fast can you build is at the speed of an idea." — Tobias Ternstrom, Lead for Database Services, AWS

Cast your mind back to 2022. It seems like a long time ago now. The world had just discovered it could have a conversation with a machine and, honestly, we were all a little amazed. You typed a question; it gave you an answer. You typed another question; it gave you another answer. It was like having the world's most-read colleague who read everything, never forgot anything, and had absolutely no idea what you asked it five minutes ago. Every conversation started from zero: no memory, no context, no continuity. And while it was genuinely useful at summarizing emails, drafting documents, and answering questions, it was fundamentally static. It couldn't learn from the conversation. It couldn't keep a thread. It couldn't connect what you told it in the morning and what you needed to know in the afternoon. It was a brilliant answer machine; it wasn't yet a thinking partner. Then in 2024, everything changed. The models didn't just get better; they got aware. For the first time, AI could hold a thread. It could remember what you told it at the start of a conversation and use it at the end, and it could carry context. This context worked not just across a prompt, but across your workflow. But memory was only half the story. AI gained the ability not just to answer questions about the world, but to reach in, query a database, check a calendar, or grab a live price. It could read a document that it had never seen before and act on what it found. It stopped being a library and became a colleague. Suddenly AI wasn't just the tool you consulted with; it was a participant in your workflow. It could take what you told it, connect it to live information, and give you something you could actually act on, not just read. The gap between insight and action had started to close because memory and tools still required you to drive. You still had to ask the right questions and direct every step. But what if AI could do that itself? Now we reach 2026. We're no longer talking about a tool you use; we're talking about a partner that works. In the Age of Agents, AI doesn't wait to be asked. It reasons, it plans, it executes, and, critically, it learns. Every interaction, every outcome, and every piece of feedback makes it sharper, more aligned, and more valuable. Think about that journey. In 2022, you asked it a question and hoped for a useful answer. In 2024, it remembered what you told it and reached into the world. And in 2026, you give it a goal and it figures out the rest. That is not an incremental improvement; that is a fundamental shift in what that means for your organization. The question is no longer "Can AI do this?" The question is "Are you building on the right foundation?" So what does that actually look like in practice? This is the AWS Agent Stack, and I want to talk about it from the ground up. At the foundation, we have SageMaker and Compute: the raw infrastructure that makes everything possible. This is purpose-built silicon optimized for AI workloads at scale. This is where the horsepower is. Above that, we have Bedrock, our managed foundation layer, with access to the world's leading models. We have AgentCore for enterprise deployment, built-in guardrails, knowledge bases, and model evaluation. These are the capabilities your agents need, ready to go. Then we have our Agent Builders that turn the tools into capabilities and into reality. At the top, we have our agent-enabled applications and frontier models. This is where customers really feel the difference, where technology becomes the experience. And wrapping all of that is Security. Security is not a feature or a final check. Security is the architecture, built into the foundation, not added at the end. Because in the Age of Agents, the organizations that lead aren't just the ones that move fastest; they are the ones that move fastest with confidence. That confidence starts with security. And so we kind of started to evolve into a performance data company, and then latterly now we're kind of evolving into a performance AI company. And it's because of the richness of the information in that data. To explain how powerful that can be, here's an example. This is a team, and they maybe they had a successful product launch project last year. If you were to take each individual aside and say, "Hey, who was the most impactful employee, the most influential employee in that project last year?" they'd probably say this person. The reason for that is their minds gravitate to the manager or the person who's standing over them, the person who talks the loudest. But if AI had a stream of data that described everything that happened in that project last year—all of how the work got done, who did what, all of the little victories—and you built AI on top of that and you were to ask AI who was the most influential person in that project last year, it might say somebody completely different. If you then were to go back to the team and say, "You know, AI says that Jill actually was the most impactful person on the project," normally they would all go, "You know what? That's actually true. We couldn't have done it without her. She was unbelievable. She made the whole thing work and she was totally instrumental." So we all kind of know who does the best work, who are the top performers, and who has the most skills. But it's about getting that knowledge out of all of the employees' heads and then serving it back to them as a real power. Now all of that can come from recognition data, but it comes from the type of recognition data from a sophisticated kind of strategic recognition program. It's not the type of recognition data that you might be used to. A lot of people have recognition programs but they're basically bots that you put in Slack where you can give somebody an emoji or "heart" somebody, and that's the extent of it. There's no information density in that type of recognition. You need structure, you need depth, and you need actual information. In the kind of recognition programs that we run for all of those big companies I mentioned, this would be a more typical recognition moment from one person to another where they write an actual paragraph. The paragraph has actual context of the work: "Thank you for your strategic insights, you brought teamwork to us, that attitude, etc. etc." And if you have that and you have thousands like them, you now can build AI tools on top of that which—and this is a thing that LLMs are marvelous at—they can take it apart and analyze all the little breadcrumbs and the little insights that are contained within that text. You can find out the people and the relationships, every person's kind of social graph in work. You can find out who they work with, which is not necessarily the org chart, because work doesn't really get done in terms of the org chart anymore; it's groupings of individuals. You can tell that from that data. You can tell what work they got done, how they perform, and the impact that they had. You can even actually tell the values of the company and the culture of the company from all of those texts. Remember you have thousands and thousands of these instances. We, as a company, have built all of these technologies on AWS infrastructure, everything from Bedrock to SageMaker. And it's a grouping of AI technologies. We have 25 years of hundreds of millions of these recognition moments. When you have that kind of volume and scale to be able to then spool up agents, spool up models, and train on massive amounts of data like that, that gives us incredible flexibility to then deploy securely all over the world. This is something that's evolving quickly. So with that, let's see a video with AWS Transform in action. Thank you. Thomson Reuters has had to evolve a lot over the last 150 plus years. With AWS, we five years ago started a migration journey to the cloud. Thomson Reuters is a global software company that specializes in information services. We've really specialized in domains and industries that have a very high reliance on trust and security. Not just a collection of scripts, not a consulting methodology, a purpose-built service with agents that reason, plan, and execute across your entire program. Wall Street Week | Fed on Iran War, AI Expectations

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·56:37 ·7.2K views · 2026-03-20
The Federal Reserve is maintaining a "higher for longer" interest rate stance due to geopolitical uncertainty and domestic fiscal stimulus, while experts suggest the AI revolution may be a gradual integration of "normal technology" rather than an immediate economic upheaval.
TL;DR The Federal Reserve is maintaining a "higher for longer" interest rate stance due to geopolitical uncertainty and domestic fiscal stimulus, while experts suggest the AI revolution may be a gradual integration of "normal technology" rather than an immediate economic...

TL;DR

The Federal Reserve is maintaining a "higher for longer" interest rate stance due to geopolitical uncertainty and domestic fiscal stimulus, while experts suggest the AI revolution may be a gradual integration of "normal technology" rather than an immediate economic upheaval.

Federal Reserve Policy and Geopolitical Uncertainty

  • Monetary Policy Outlook: Randal Quarles (Cynosure, former Fed Vice Chair) predicts no change in interest rates for the remainder of the year. While the Fed is "data-dependent," current drivers remain more inflationary than non-inflationary.
  • The Neutral Rate: The Fed’s estimate for the neutral interest rate ticked up from 3.0% to 3.1%. Quarles suggests this is an institutional "catch-up" to structural changes that have existed for some time rather than a direct result of AI investment.
  • Inflationary Pressures:
      • Energy Markets: Uncertainty surrounding the war in Iran could lead to "demand destruction" if higher prices persist beyond two months.
      • Fiscal Stimulus: The "One Big Beautiful Bill Act" provides growth incentives but is currently pushing upward on short-term inflation.
  • Labor Supply Shifts: Quarles argues the market underestimates the shrinkage in labor supply due to immigration policy changes, effectively offsetting softness in job demand and supporting higher interest rates.

The Reality of AI Integration

  • "Normal Technology" Perspective: Arvind Narayanan (Princeton Professor) argues AI is a powerful general-purpose technology similar to electricity, requiring gradual integration rather than signaling an impending superintelligence that overrides economic laws.
  • Reliability vs. Capability: High performance on benchmarks does not equal reliability. Narayanan cites the Air Canada lawsuit—where a chatbot invented a refund policy—as evidence that legal liability and organizational hurdles will slow adoption.
  • Knowledge Worker Impact:
      • Expanding Inquiry: Drew Matus (MetLife Chief Market Strategist) suggests AI doesn't eliminate tasks but allows workers to ask more complex questions, increasing their value to firms.
      • Abstraction of Labor: Historically, technology moves workers to higher levels of supervision. In software engineering, despite heavy AI tool adoption, job postings for engineers continue to increase.
  • Limits of AI: Narayanan pushes back against the idea that AI can solve geopolitical strategy, noting that the inability to predict the future is a data and agency limitation, not a biological one.

U.S.-Canada Trade Relations

  • Economic Strain: Following President Trump’s tariffs, Canada has seen real GDP contract in two of the four quarters of 2025, with annual growth slowing to 1.7%.
  • The "Trust Gap": Over half of Canadians polled no longer view the U.S. as a reliable ally. Author Louise Penny noted she redirected her book tours to Canada in protest, citing "ruinous" tariffs on consumer goods.
  • Divergent Business Views:
      • Investment Chill: Goldy Hyder (Canada Business Council) reports that capital is "frozen" in trade-sensitive sectors like steel and forestry.
      • Contrarian View: Barry Zekelman (CEO of Zekelman Industries) argues that while he pays $7 million monthly in tariffs, U.S. demand is "booming," and Canada should endorse similar pro-growth policies rather than "poking the U.S. in the eye" at global forums.

Social Media Regulation: The Australia Experiment

  • 100-Day Review: Australia’s ban on social media for those under 16 has seen roughly five million accounts removed across 10 platforms.
  • Implementation Challenges: Paul Litherland (Surf Online Safe) expresses concern that the "reasonable steps" required by legislation are being loosely interpreted by tech companies, allowing children to circumvent bans via new accounts.
  • The Business Case for Youth:
      • Snapchat Operations: Jennifer Park Stout (SVP, Snap) reports the company removed 400,000 users in Australia (out of 8 million daily users). She argues blanket bans ignore the "sweet spot" of community connection.
      • Long-term Growth: Ravi Iyer (USC Marshall) notes that while youth aren't the primary source of current revenue, banning them disrupts "network effects" and habit formation, which could hurt the long-term growth engines that drive stock prices.
"The usual reasons that are given for why AI is going to displace labor rely on a whole bunch of fallacies... Capability is not enough. You have to have reliability." — Arvind Narayanan, Princeton University
"Capital doesn't have a heart. Canadians like Americans, I think Americans like Canadians, and we'll get through this [trade war]." — Goldy Hyder, Canada Business Council
"I think the argument for lowering interest rates is going to be hard to carry, probably for the rest of this year." — Randal Quarles, Cynosure
"Suppose that some telescope had seen an alien invasion fleet that was going to get here in about 10 years. We would be scared... We're constructing these aliens [AI], but they're going to get here in about 10 years and they're going to be smarter than us." — Geoffrey Hinton, "Godfather of AI"
This is Wall Street Week. I'm David Westin, bringing you stories of capitalism. We've heard all about how AI will change our world in ways we have yet to imagine. But we were surprised to find another side of the argument: a leading expert who thinks we may be getting it all wrong. And President Trump hasn't made too many friends in Canada during his second term. We go north of the border to get Canadians' perspectives on what's happened to America's other special relationship. Plus, Australia has led the way in imposing age limits on social media. 100 days in, how are the country's new restrictions affecting children and families? And how big is the risk for social media companies if they lose their younger users? But we start with the Fed and how it handled the uncertainties posed by the Iran war when it met this week. Randal Quarles of Cynosure served as Fed vice chair. We heard from the Federal Reserve and from Chair Powell this week, and maybe the headline is the uncertainty because of the war in Iran. He was very careful in saying: we don't know what the effects will be—big, small, or in between. How does one make policy in the face of that kind of uncertainty? Well, you know, at writ large, that's the job of the Fed generally. The Fed is always in a position of uncertainty. That uncertainty may be greater from moment to moment. It's obviously at a fairly high level now. But that's really what's behind the Fed's regular mantra of: we'll be data-dependent. The data could be different next meeting than they are today. On the basis of what we know now, this is where we would be likely to go. I think, however, when you're at a level of uncertainty like this, you have to look at what you think the fundamental drivers of the economy are likely to be as you think going forward. And I think that those drivers currently are more inflationary than non-inflationary and will therefore probably argue for probably no change in the Federal Reserve's monetary policy stance for some time into the future. I don't think that where the economy is going is going to argue for raising interest rates. But I think the argument for lowering interest rates is going to be hard to carry, probably for the rest of this year. On the uncertainty, just for one more moment on that: at what point does the uncertainty itself start to weigh on the economy? Because businesses might be reluctant to make investments, consumers may hold off on spending. How long can we go before we start to see it, you think, in the economy? Oh, well, I think you start seeing that relatively quickly in terms of lowered business investment because you don't know exactly what environment you're investing into. We saw that at Liberation Day. There was a significant pause in business investment until companies felt that they had their foot on a rock with regard to where tariff policy was likely to go. And so you'll see that again now. The question is: how long does it last before that becomes a sort of material drag on the economy? Tell us about demand destruction. I'm reading about that a lot these days, which is not something you want, necessarily. What are the risks of that and at what point might we start seeing demand destruction given what's going on, particularly with the energy markets? Again, you're going to start seeing responses pretty quickly to higher energy prices in consumer spending. And again, because of the uncertainty about the future macro-environment and business investment. If that's relatively short, if we're talking a resolution in a month, even in two months, then that's, from the point of view of the Fed, which looks over long arcs of time, a blip. If it goes on longer than that, you know, then you'll start to see a reaction. But on the flip side of that, which is partly why I think you're not going to see a hike in interest rates, but you're not going to see lower interest rates either: those are effects that slow the economy. But higher energy prices feed into higher prices generally and that's inflationary. I think a lot of the other drivers of the economy, the fiscal stimulus that you have from the One Big Beautiful Bill Act, which I thought was excellent legislation, provides the right incentives for the economy, but it is stimulative and pushing upward on inflation in the short term. So I think the balance over the course of the next several months is likely to be in favor of no moves either down or up. One of the things we heard from the Fed this week was that the neutral interest rate has ticked up a little bit, I think from 3.0 to 3.1. What is driving that? Is it some of the factors you just suggested, or is it some other structural things such as AI investment? I don't think that AI investment is driving a change currently in the neutral interest rate. I think it's too early for us to see that sort of an effect. I think it's more the factors that I was talking about earlier. And I think part of this, too, is that the Fed is beginning to recognize changes in the overall environment that have been pushing upward on the neutral interest rate for some time. I think their sort of institutional estimate of the neutral interest rate has been lower than warranted for some time and they're catching up to where the actual neutral rate has been for a while. We saw in the Fed, in the summary of economic projections, both a slight increase in core inflation, but also in growth. Where does that growth come from, do you think at this point? I think you'll see growth across the economy. I don't think that, for example, the sole driver of growth is AI investment or AI changes in business processes. Again, I think we're too early for that. But the economic policies of this administration have been quite supportive of growth, and in particular the fiscal policy of the One Big Beautiful Bill Act. And those effects will be felt throughout the economy. And the elements of the administration's economic policy package, which at the outset of the administration one might have thought could have slowed growth, for example, immigration policy, maybe tariff policy, have ended up being not as slowing of growth as one expected at the beginning. So of the overall administration economic policy package, which I think was much more coherent and well thought out than a lot of people give it credit for, I think that the stimulative elements are outweighing the potential growth-slowing elements, and so as a consequence, we'll see that stimulation across the economy. You mentioned tariffs and goodness knows there's been a lot of talk about tariffs. What I took away from what Chair Powell and the Fed said was basically they do increase prices for a time being, but it's usually eight months to a year, and that'll be up sometime in the middle of the year. What are you seeing out there in the real world? Is that consistent with what you're seeing? Yes, it's very consistent. There's a slightly higher risk that tariffs could feed into overall inflation expectations just because of how this tariff policy has been rolled out, because it's been up, it's been down, it's been rolled out over a long period of time as opposed to: develop, explain, implement, with a delay for everybody to process it. That would be the typical way to implement tariff policy. And then it would be very clear that you have kind of a one-time step change in prices during the period of implementation of the tariff policy that shouldn't feed into overall inflation expectations. There's a slightly higher risk that you push up inflation generally for a longer period of time just because of the way that tariff policy was rolled out. But I don't think it's terribly high and I think you can expect the Fed can kind of expect to look through tariff policy. The tariffs themselves are not nearly as high as people thought that they might be at the outset of the administration. You mentioned immigration policy and it did come up in the news conference with Chair Powell in this sense. He said, look, we still have sort of a low-hire, low-fire labor economy right now, and therefore the unemployment rate actually is not going up. But he said that's not a really great balance. What are the risks in that sort of employment market? So I actually think that people are underestimating the degree to which the labor supply has been changed as a result of immigration policy. You know, the administration hasn't really succeeded in forcibly deporting a very many people. That's hard to do. The Eisenhower administration tried to do something similar and had sort of similar results. But the policy had the effect of almost immediately closing off a very large amount of illegal immigration that there had been during previous years, a certain amount of self-deportation, and even legal immigration is down materially. So the growth in the labor supply that we had during the Biden administration has been closed off by a very significant amount, really maybe between one and two million people a year, I think. And so the softness that you see in the labor demand numbers, in the jobs numbers, I think people are underestimating the degree to which that is effectively offset or significantly offset by the shrinkage in labor supply. You layer on top of that just the continuing aging of the baby boomers. And so I am less concerned, and I think the data that you described are supportive of that lower level of concern than the Fed has been expressing for some time about the state of the labor market. And I think as the data continue to evolve, that will be another sort of support for interest rate policy that is on the higher end rather than the lower end of expectations. This is a story about right-sizing our expectations. Since ChatGPT burst on the scene four years ago, all we've heard about is how big AI will be: how it could lead to superintelligence replacing humans, how fast it is coming, and at least for some, how dangerous it may be. We have 2,000 people doing it. We spend 2 billion dollars a year on it. It affects everything: risk, fraud, marketing, idea generation, customer service, and it's kind of the tip of the iceberg. Imagine 100 million people smarter than any Nobel Prize winner. When AI gets superintelligent, it might just replace us. But what if they're wrong? What if AI isn't taking us toward either utopia or dystopia? What if AI is just normal? I have been surprised by how controversial the statement "AI is normal technology" is. Arvind Narayanan is a professor of computer science and director of the Center for Information Technology Policy at Princeton. He's also co-author of the book "AI Snake Oil: What Artificial Intelligence Can Do, What It Can't, and How to Tell the Difference." We acknowledge that AI is a powerful general-purpose technology. Compare it to electricity, the internet, I think even the industrial revolution. But like all of those technologies, what we think is happening and will continue to happen is a gradual integration of this technology into society. If it is gradual, corporate America doesn't seem to be investing that way. The six US firms pouring the most cash into AI are projected to spend over 750 billion dollars on it this year alone, more than the entire GDP of Ireland. And some firms are already claiming significant layoffs are driven by AI. But Narayanan is more circumspect. This is not, we think, some impending superintelligence that is going to, you know, render obsolete the laws of economics or the limitations of human behavior. The usual reasons that are given for why AI is going to displace labor rely on a whole bunch of fallacies. AI developers are looking at these so-called capability benchmarks. Oh, you know, AI is as capable as people are at answering customer service questions, so surely it's going to replace customer service representatives. Maybe. But what we said was: hey, let's look at reliability separately from capability. Capability is not enough. You have to have reliability, which means: does it answer the same question reliably every time, or is it giving different answers to different customers? Does it know which tasks it can take on and which ones are out of scope? Drew Matus, the chief market strategist at MetLife, agrees. I think when you think about what artificial intelligence does and why people are concerned about it is they're afraid that the knowledge worker is going to become extinct. But the reality of it is that knowledge workers are constantly using technology to expand the bounds of knowledge and when they expand those bounds, then they have other questions they need to ask. If I have 100 questions that I start the day with and technology can help me answer 50 of them very quickly, it's not like I have 50 left. That just created 50 new questions for me to answer. And the more questions I answer, the more valuable I become, and the more valuable I become, the more valuable the company I work for becomes. Some companies are investing hundreds of billions of dollars right now in AI. They have to get a return on that investment from somewhere. Either they have to actually have more revenues and more profits, or they have to cut their costs somewhere, and that's part of the concern I think about employment. Some people say: yeah, you've got to cut employment. How does that pencil out, do you think? How does a return on investment for all that come? Well, so I think that that's a very short-term way of looking at the world. And I think that if we really consider the long-term view of what's best for a lot of companies, it's to make the investment and make sure that you understand where the payoff is. Recently, companies have justified layoffs by saying AI can replace workers. But some critics have countered by calling the move "AI washing," implying that companies are dressing up old-fashioned cost-cutting as AI adoption in a bid to satisfy shareholders. What we've seen historically is that as technology advances, people move to higher and higher levels of abstraction, of performance: work that involves supervising the technology to do the work instead of doing it directly themselves. And what we're seeing with AI so far is consistent with this pattern. So my bet is on not seeing massive labor effects across the economy, but there might be specific jobs and sectors in which there might, in fact, be quite negative consequences. Software engineering has been the clear leader, I think, in the pace of AI adoption, but also the effects of AI adoption. To the point where going back to a time when all the code was written manually by hand, that almost feels like going back to, you know, punch cards before the days of keyboards. Critically, though, even in companies where AI is being rapidly adopted for software engineering, it's not really clear that it's leading to replacing software engineers with AI. In fact, the number of job postings for software engineers actually continues to increase. Until recently, job postings for software engineers and others had been going down, but last year the need for programmers spiked, unlike the rest of the jobs market. One of the things that is said is that although it may be as profound as the internet or electricity, it's coming much faster. Does that make a difference? There are many claims that are being made that AI is the fastest adopted technology in history. But when we looked at those numbers when writing this essay, we weren't really convinced. If you look at something that could really make an economic impact, like replacing customer service representatives with chatbots, for instance. I mean, when ChatGPT came out, so many people including me thought that that was the first thing that was going to happen in terms of labor effects. Chatbot, it's right there in the name. Why is that still not happening for the most part? It turns out that when you kind of look at this deeper AI integration where there are risks, there are legal liabilities involved, there are structural and organizational changes that companies have to make: it's not so simple. One of the stories we heard was that Air Canada had this kind of customer service chatbot, and it made up a nonexistent refund policy when a customer was asking about it. The customer got upset and sued. It went all the way to the Canadian Supreme Court. And what the court decided was to force the airline to abide by this nonexistent refund policy. The speed limits in many cases are things like regulatory barriers that have, of course, been inserted by humans. But for very good reason: there are, you know, there's kind of a saying that every regulation is written in blood, every safety regulation at least. So when you look at why AI can't make rapid inroads into healthcare, for instance: it's because we're not going to let AI autonomously do medical experiments on people to figure out how to cure cancer. Samyukta Mullangi has seen that firsthand. She's an oncologist who has been hired as Vice President of Clinical Strategy at OpenEvidence, a medical chatbot that was recently valued at 12 billion dollars and has been called the ChatGPT for doctors. I'm a little bit circumspect when it comes to those grandiose statements around AI tools just replacing physicians entirely. But I do think that there's definitely going to be, you know, a world in which AI tools become a part of clinical practice. And I'm excited to enter that world, to be honest. I certainly do not think that they're going to replace physicians. Every single company founder putting out statements talking about the efficacy of their tool, they'll say something like: this tool has outperformed a group of physicians in coming to a diagnosis or solving a clinical problem. And I think what they're trying to do by sort of putting these statements out there entirely is showcase the efficacy of their tool and as well not take any liability for the downside that could happen because the AI tool has hallucinated or provided a biased answer or provided a false negative. And I think that's a real problem. I think it's actually really inappropriate to sort of make statements like that without kind of realizing sort of assigning or taking on responsibility for what happens when your algorithm messes up. What happens if a physician takes on a recommendation that an algorithm has generated and that turns out to be the wrong one? Who kind of, like, assumes that responsibility? Like most technology, AI will continue to improve. And even if it doesn't get everything right now, that potential for improvement leads some to worry that it might get too smart for humans to handle. Among those raising concerns is Geoffrey Hinton, a Nobel prize-winning computer scientist known as the godfather of AI. Suppose that some telescope had seen an alien invasion fleet that was going to get here in about 10 years. We would be scared and we would be doing stuff about it. Well, that's what we have. We're constructing these aliens, but they're going to get here in about 10 years and they're going to be smarter than us. We should be thinking very, very hard: how are we going to coexist with these things? What do you say to the people who say there may actually be a greater danger here beyond just losing your job? Where I disagree with people like Hinton is on two big things. One: if we just club a whole bunch of things together as some umbrella category of AI risk and then treat it as an AI problem, we just lose a lot of clarity and we lose a lot of avenues by which we can actually address the problem. So one kind of risk that people are worried about is that AI is very good at hacking, finding new vulnerabilities in software, and maybe taking over critical systems. That is something we should be worried about. But it turns out that specific risk has a very specific solution. But this idea that we should imbue AI with a maternal instinct, or in computer science, it goes by the term "alignment," that AI is going to magically know what is the right thing to do in every possible situation: that seems like a pipe dream to me, because what is the right thing to do in every possible situation? Well, people don't agree on that, so how can we agree on what AI should do in those situations? And so putting all our eggs in this one basket of alignment is going to result in a very brittle scenario, which is that if anyone ever creates, you know, a misaligned AI system, then all bets are off. And how are you ever going to stop every kid in the world from creating their own AI system that might not follow your rules or your instinct or whatever it is? Because the trend we've seen is that something that takes a data center today, within a few years is going to be something that you can do in your mom's basement. Whatever this new world of AI makes possible, Narayanan says there's one thing that it simply will not be able to do: predict the future. Once this happens, we will have no choice but to rely on AI in order to figure out what, let's say, military or geopolitical strategy should be. But when we actually look at the research, the picture that emerges is that the reason people are not that great at predicting the future is not some limitation of our biology. It's because the data that's out there that might allow us to extrapolate to what might happen in the future is pretty limited, but also the future is genuinely unknown. And just because the future is genuinely unknown, none of us, not the experts like Hinton and Narayanan, and not even AI itself, can predict just how big it can get or how fast it can grow. Up next, it takes a lot to get the Canadians angry. Has President Trump managed to do it this time? This is a story about a troubled relationship. For 100 years, the United States has had close ties with its neighbor to the north. But when President Trump imposed tariffs on Canada last year, Ottawa responded. Businesses braced, and the tone between the two neighbors shifted from familiar to fraught. For a lot of Canadians, this wasn't just about prices or profits; it was about trust and whether the US still sees Canada as a partner and ally. Michael McKee takes us to Canada to see how the relationship looks from north of the border. An hour north of the line dividing the US and Canada, in a small town called Knowlton, the cross-border relationship feels fragile. We love the American people, but we're also defensive about our economy and want to support Canadian business, which we feel might be under attack right now. Having family in the United States as well makes things a little precarious right now. Since President Trump imposed new tariffs on imports from Canada, the relationship has soured. Canadian imports from the US are down, and the nation slipped from being the largest buyer of US goods to number two, behind Mexico. Canadians know that our old comfortable assumptions that our geography and alliance memberships automatically confer prosperity and security, that assumption is no longer valid. And we are rapidly diversifying abroad. The past few days we've concluded new strategic partnerships with China and Qatar. Although Carney is optimistic about Canada's economic agenda, February's jobs report paints a bleaker picture. The country shed the most jobs in more than four years last month, sending unemployment up to 6.7% from 6.5% in January. We're all going to lose around 2% of our GDP because of the new trade environment. That includes the United States. When I say 2%, for somebody it's 100% of their livelihood. Stephen Poloz succeeded Mark Carney as Governor of the Bank of Canada and is now a special advisor at Canadian business law firm Osler. Let's talk a little bit more about the loss. Where do we see it now? Oh, you see it in autos, you see it in steel, you see it in aluminum, you see it in copper, and you see it in the forestry sector. These are sectors that are deemed really strategically important. As US tariffs weighed on Canadian exports for much of the year, real GDP contracted in two of the four quarters of 2025, and for the year increased by just 1.7%, the slowest pace of annual growth since the economy shrank during COVID in 2020. Right now you still encounter that belligerence at the grocery store. You're looking for the thing not grown in the United States, or, oh look, Mexican vegetable versus US, California. You know, people are still doing that. Some Canadians say they feel betrayed by the US, with more than half of respondents in a political poll saying the country is no longer a reliable ally. Life here before this particular round was wonderful, we had a great relationship. I guess it was February of last year when everything changed. Louise Penny is a Canadian journalist turned author. When she was on tour last year, Penny decided to redirect her US tour dates back to Canada in protest of Trump's tariffs. She's also refusing to return to the US until the trade war is over, a trend that's becoming increasingly common amongst Canadians. The decision to not tour in the United States was obvious. It wasn't even a decision. There was no way I was going to go into a country that had declared war on us, any more than I would imagine Americans if Canada had done the same thing to you. We sell merchandise here and the vast majority of it went into the United States, now no longer. Not because people don't want it, but because the tariffs are ruinous. They can't buy a 30-dollar mug and have 50 dollars worth of tariffs put on it. So we've lost huge amounts because of the tariffs. And we're small; I can only imagine what larger corporations in Canada are suffering. What are CEOs saying? What are they thinking at this point? Well, they remain very cautious, I'd say. In areas where trade matters the most, they're essentially still frozen. And there the type would be just: keep the lights on, do our maintenance spend, keep our powder dry. The truth is we need to be more competitive, we need to be more productive. This is not a unique problem to Canada. Goldy Hyder is the CEO of the Canada Business Council. Prime Minister Carney always likes to emphasize that when it comes to the marginal effective tax rate, the Canadian rate is the lowest in the G7. Those kinds of things inspire confidence for businesses to be able to deploy capital. Otherwise, you're going to see a lot of chilling and freezing of the capital. Although small business owners and some industry CEOs are feeling the pinch, some have a more nuanced view. All those policies are working, more so than I've ever seen in my 40-year career. That's why I'd like Canada to endorse and do the same thing. Barry Zekelman is the chief executive of Zekelman Industries, a steel pipe and tube manufacturer. He sees the relationship from both sides of the border, having started his business in Windsor, Ontario, now having his headquarters in Chicago and producing his products in both countries. How do the tariffs affect your business in Canada? Tremendously. I'm paying massive tariffs to ship products from Canada into the US. Can you put some kind of dollar figure or percentage figure on what you're having to pay? We're having to pay roughly almost 250, 270 dollars a ton, US. So, you know, I'm paying 6-7 million dollars a month in tariffs. It's huge. You know, we've got to eat it. I've got to keep this plant running. I'm trying. My US customers need the product. I'm manufacturing more and more in the US, we've upped our production there tremendously. I mean, if you had to compare Canada to the US, the US is booming. I have never seen more robust demand. So the policies are working over there. So the back and forth on tariffs, even though a lot is covered under USMCA, has put a chill on the Canadian economy? Yeah. We need to resolve it. I understand Prime Minister Carney, and he's our Prime Minister, so I've got to stand behind the leadership of our country. But we have to have a healthy trading relationship with the US. It is not going to be replaced by other countries. We can't just keep poking them in the eye and telling them we don't need you and standing there at Davos pretending that these mid-countries are going to band together against the US. Every one of them is in the back door trying to sign a deal with the US. It's not going to happen. It's the greatest economy in the world, it's the market that everybody in the world wants to be in. And we're right next door to it with the longest border in the world next to it and the biggest trading relationship, and we're saying: oh, we're going to move on? That doesn't work. That doesn't hold water. When you sit there and say: oh, we're going to make it the 51st state. Look, people got to relax and people have to calm down. You don't feel insulted by the President as a Canadian? No. I've got thicker skin. I mean, you can't get insulted like that and turn that into a global trading fight. You know, read in between the lines. I'm telling you, I believe with everything in my heart that if President Donald Trump had the right relationship with Canada, a fair trading relationship, and resolved some of these issues, he'd put his arms around Canada and hug them and welcome them into the family because he would feel secure that we've got a family that's working together for the greater good of North America against many bad actors who are bad for the US and bad for Canada. I think it will be an important component of his legacy if we're able to review and renew it and allow it to continue to bring about the prosperity and the growth and the security that it has offered for decades now. What we cannot have is an agreement that some have called a "zombie agreement": it exists but it doesn't really mean anything, it just kind of perpetuates out there in the abyss. That's not constructive. And let me be clear about one thing that your viewers of more than anywhere else in the world would understand: capital doesn't have a heart. Canadians like Americans, I think Americans like Canadians, and we'll get through this. Coming up, putting an age limit on social media users. Australia's done it. What would it mean if the rest of us followed? This is a story about trying to put the genie back in the bottle. It's been 100 days since Australia implemented a world-leading policy restricting social media for those under the age of 16. In the months since, other countries have moved toward similar measures. But while few argue about the need to do what we can to protect the mental health of young people, opinion is split on the success of the new rule. Long before Australia's social media restrictions took effect, Sydney father of five Dany Elachi knew his eldest daughter's mobile phone was causing more harm than good. She constantly would say to us that if we didn't give her a phone, she'd be the only child in her grade without one, she would lose all her social connection. So we did give in and gave her that phone. Within the space of days, we saw that it ripped her away from the family life. It completely overwhelmed all her spare time. She was no longer reading like she was previously, she was no longer engaging with her siblings, she wasn't helping with dinner anymore. Every spare moment was spent attending to those pings and dings. It was enough to prompt Elachi to start a community group, Heads Up Alliance, for Australian parents delaying social media and smartphones for their children. So when the Australian government announced a landmark new policy: This is Australia showing enough is enough. Elachi was relieved. The social media laws that came into effect in December were a huge vindication for our movement. Because for a long time we were spreading the word through word of mouth, and, you know, some people thought that we were being a little bit over the top, that perhaps we weren't with the times.
