Jared Friedman (Partner, Y Combinator) Gaurab Chakrabarti (Co-founder, Solugen) Sean Hunt (Co-founder, Solugen)
> "Capital constraint forces very creative thinking...
TL;DR: Solugen is disrupting the $1 trillion chemical industry by replacing fossil-fuel-based manufacturing with a "chemoenzymatic" process that uses sugar feedstock and a breakthrough enzyme found in pancreatic cancer to produce chemicals more efficiently, safely, and...

TL;DR: Solugen is disrupting the $1 trillion chemical industry by replacing fossil-fuel-based manufacturing with a "chemoenzymatic" process that uses sugar feedstock and a breakthrough enzyme found in pancreatic cancer to produce chemicals more efficiently, safely, and sustainably.

The Core Innovation: Chemoenzymatic Processing

  • The Hybrid Model: Solugen fuses biology and chemistry by pairing the specificity of enzymes with the scale of traditional metal catalysts.
      • Yield Efficiency: While traditional chemical reactions often see a 60% yield, Solugen’s process achieves a 96% yield at scale.
      • The "Eureka" Discovery: Co-founder Gaurab Chakrabarti discovered a mutated enzyme in pancreatic cancer cells that produces high concentrations of hydrogen peroxide to shield the cancer from the immune system. This enzyme became the engine for Solugen’s first industrial products.
  • Sustainable Feedstock: The process replaces oil and gas feedstocks with corn syrup (sugar).
      • Clean Output: Because the process starts with organic sugar rather than hydrocarbons, it eliminates many of the toxic byproducts associated with traditional chemical plants.

Scrappy Scaling and Capital Efficiency

  • The $7,000 Prototype: Eschewing the traditional "raise big, build big" hard tech model, the founders built their first reactor using PVC pipes from Home Depot and a Walmart shelf.
      • Revenue First: This DIY reactor generated $12,000/month in revenue before the company ever raised significant venture capital.
  • Modular Manufacturing: Solugen’s current plant, "Bioforge 1," was built in five separate locations and assembled on-site in Houston like Lego blocks.
      • Scalability: The current 60-foot tall reactor is an identical, scaled-up version of the original PVC pipe design—moving from 7 gallons to 10,000 gallons.
      • In-House Production: To maintain vertical integration, Solugen operates its own biology and metals labs to produce proprietary enzymes and catalysts.

Customer-Centric Go-to-Market Strategy

  • Finding the "Wedge": During their time at Y Combinator, the founders bypassed complex industrial supply chains by selling peroxide directly to float spa and hot tub owners in Dallas.
      • Disruption via Distribution: By selling directly, they undercut the massive retail markups on the "small brown bottles" of 3% peroxide found in stores.
  • Hyper-Targeted Marketing: To land their first major oil and gas contract, the founders identified the single individual who controlled chemical spending at a target company.
      • The Billboard Hack: They rented cheap billboards along the specific highway route the executive used for his daily commute, creating the illusion of a massive brand presence.
  • The "Customer PhD": The founders credit YC for teaching them to become "PhDs in their customers," allowing them to build only what the market was ready to pay for.

Future Outlook: Rebuilding American Manufacturing

  • Local Production: Solugen builds factories near its customers to minimize shipping costs and logistics complexity, proving that high-tech manufacturing is viable in the U.S.
  • Efficiency at Scale: The process is now so refined that a single "Coke bottle" worth of enzyme can produce two to four tanker trucks of finished chemical product.
  • Diverse Applications: While they started with hydrogen peroxide, Solugen now produces chemicals for water treatment, national defense, infrastructure, agriculture, and skincare.

***

Notable Quotes

"Capital constraint forces very creative thinking... with 10 grand, you have a very confined space of what you can afford to buy to try to make the product. And so ours ended up being PVC, like a Walmart shelf." — Sean Hunt (Co-founder & CTO)
"The second you have a PhD in your customer and you're an expert in their world, then you know exactly what you can and can't build. It's that simple." — Gaurab Chakrabarti (Co-founder & CEO)
"We feed in one Coke bottle of enzyme and you get two to four tanker trucks of product. That's how efficient the enzyme is." — Sean Hunt (Co-founder & CTO)
This reactor in Houston, Texas represents a whole new approach to chemical manufacturing. We make chemicals in a completely new way. We started off by making hydrogen peroxide; now we sell products for water treatment, national defense, infrastructure, agriculture—you name it, we make a chemical for it. The story of Solugen is as scrappy as it gets. It all started with this prototype, built out of PVC pipes the founders bought from Home Depot. In its heyday, this did 12,000 bucks a month in revenue. Fast forward to now, Solugen is a billion-dollar company shipping out tanker trucks of products that power critical US industries. So how did they go from this single beaker of hydrogen peroxide to this full-scale manufacturing plant? I visited Solugen HQ in Houston, Texas to find out. What do you guys do here? What is Solugen? We use biology to create chemicals that allow us to create smaller chemical plants and have a cleaner, safer, more environmentally friendly footprint. We've invented a process called chemoenzymatic processing, where we take the specificity of biology by taking an enzyme and pairing it with a metal catalyst, which is what's traditionally used in industry. What we've done with these is by marrying these two, you can actually create more efficient reactions. So instead of having a 60% yield, you can have a 96% yield, which is what we have at scale, precisely because of these two catalysts. Solugen is the first company to fuse biology and chemistry in this way, pulling enzymes from living cells—in this case corn syrup—and pairing them with novel metal catalysts. The output is chemicals that can be used in everything from agriculture to skincare. Traditionally, chemical plants relied on fossil fuel feedstock, which has led to all kinds of unintended consequences. This new approach is cleaner, safer, and more efficient. We receive rail cars of corn syrup, and then we run our utility system. We change the parameters of the plant, the way that the enzymes are reacting, and the way that the metal catalysts are reacting to oxidize the corn syrup how we want. At the end, we evaporate water, take the final products, and store them in finished good tanks, or we send them to a blend farm to get blended with other chemicals. That's the process. Solugen started with a true eureka moment. It was the kind of freak invention that normally happens only in science fiction. Sean was working in the chemicals industry and Gaurab was in medical school studying pancreatic cancer. I was working on this skunkworks project to try to do direct hydrogen peroxide synthesis, where you react hydrogen and oxygen gas directly together over a metal catalyst. And then, you know, I was working on this project and then Gaurab was like, "Oh yeah no, I found this really crazy mechanism in pancreatic cancer where there's locally 50% hydrogen peroxide concentration." I was like, "What? That's wild." It turned out that an obscure enzyme found in pancreatic cancer cells that Gaurab was studying was the key to a new process for making industrial hydrogen peroxide. So these pancreatic cancer cells, they put out peroxide. Peroxide creates almost like an invisibility cloak around the pancreatic cancer that makes it difficult for immune cells to come in. And so I was like, "That's a very interesting discovery, but why?" Once we asked the question why, we found out the reason was an enzyme. It was just a mutated enzyme that's found only in pancreatic cancer. And so that's when we said, "What if the two worlds could collide? What if enzymes and metal catalysts could coexist?" Here's how it works. Solugen feeds corn syrup to the enzymes, the same ones from the pancreatic cancer cells, which transform it into new compounds. They then use metal catalysts to further process those into the final chemicals that can be used to build all kinds of products we use in our everyday lives. In most chemical plants, the feedstock comes from oil and gas, which inevitably produces toxic byproducts. Solugen literally starts with sugar. We had to suspend belief for a second. People believe that anything to do with biology and chemicals is just a bad mix, because biology is too sensitive, it's gonna break down, blah blah blah blah blah. But we said let's suspend that criticism for a second and just look at the numbers. We said if we can make the enzyme last this long on stream and the product is this concentration, we can make a lot of money. And that's where we started. This insight that organic enzymes could operate at industrial scale and efficiency was the company's first key breakthrough. Today, Solugen operates both a biology and metals lab, where it produces its own enzymes and metal catalysts in house. These are actually enzymatic reactors. So what we do is we grow bugs, we break the bugs open, we take the enzymes and we put them in these reactors, and we can stress them out and figure out what they're capable of doing at scale. We have outfitted this with probably some of the best analytics that you can ever have for enzymes, which gives us a good insight into how things will scale. Across from the biology lab is Solugen's metals lab. We just went to the enzyme lab. We basically now pair that enzyme with the right metal and so you can basically mix and match which metal and which enzyme you want to pair together. Their next big insight was a commercial one. Previous startups that had tried to do something like this all started by raising a huge amount of funding and building a large-scale plant. Solugen took a different approach. They built their first reactor for just $10,000 and then started selling to customers almost immediately. Gradually, they scaled up to larger and larger plants. So, you did this techno-economic analysis and you're like, "Wait a second, this could actually work." And so May 2016 we got unofficial second place in the MIT 100k competition. We lost. We lost gloriously but we got 10,000 bucks. $10,000—that doesn't seem like very much money. I think capital constraint forces very creative thinking because with 10 grand, you have a very confined space of what you can afford to buy to try to make the product. And so ours ended up being PVC, like a Walmart shelf. You know, we couldn't even afford the metal catalyst part, so it was just the enzyme portion. This was the very first Solugen reactor? Yep, this is the first one, complete with schedule 80 PVC from Home Depot. This is like a bubble column with a membrane. So you sparge air into the bottom; inside there's the liquid with the corn syrup and the enzyme and it's kind of spinning in a loop and then they're reacting, which makes the peroxide. Then this is a membrane, and so the membrane keeps the enzyme in the bubble column and then the permeate on the membrane is the peroxide product. Armed with just their $10,000 reactor but no customers, Sean and Gaurab applied and were accepted into YC. But they deferred a few months and set out to first try and sell the tiny volumes of peroxide they could make. We made our first product in like September 2016. We couldn't afford any controls, right? So this is a total manual operation. We'd come in in the morning, we would try to get this reactor to a steady state, and then I'd go to work and he'd go to the hospital. He was in his last year of med school. I was on surgery rotation. He was on surgery rotation. Wow. 36-hour shifts. Good times. And then in the evenings we'd go in and try to re-tune it to a steady state. Our first three customers were float spa hot tub owners in Dallas. We discovered the supply chain dislocations because for these hot tub owners, they were buying 3% peroxide in the brown bottle on the store. That went through multiple distributors, multiple downpackers, put in little brown bottles, shipping to the store, retail markup. It's like, oh, we only have 10 grand but we're actually manufacturing the chemical and we're bypassing huge distribution value chains. So on weekends we would pour the chemicals in people's hot tubs. We looked at a bunch of markets to be like, "Where can we just wedge ourselves into?" When we first accepted Solugen into YC, they had zero revenue, but they deferred by a batch and spent six months getting those first customers. By the time they started at YC, they were gaining traction. Typically when people think about hard tech companies like you guys and YC, they're like, "That makes no sense. What could you possibly accomplish for a few hundred thousand dollars?" To most people it intuitively seems hilariously mismatched to the cost and timelines of something like a new chemical plant. This fundamentally goes back to that customer experience, which is what YC taught us to do. It was really like grad school for customers. That's how I look at YC, where the second you have a PhD in your customer and you're an expert in their world, then you know exactly what you can and can't build. It's that simple. If you know this customer is not the right fit, that's okay, go to the next customer. I remember the picture of the beaker because in your demo video, that's what it was. Do you remember this? The blue beaker and then we had the color change back and forth with the metal catalyst. You totally understood the reason that this would work, you'd worked out all the techno-economics—all the math was done and the idea was fully flushed out. I don't think the core idea has changed one bit since the blue beaker phase. Literally the same. Yeah, it's just like a million times larger. Exactly, it's just larger. During YC, you guys are still driving around with buckets of peroxide and selling them to spas. You finish YC, you raise your $4 million seed round, what happens then? So we move to where our customers were, which was Houston, Texas, and we used the seed capital to build our first big pilot reactor—not just the PVC pipe reactor but a proper 1,500 gallon type reactor. And then our first oil and gas field trial was January 2018. And so we had billboards targeting this guy so we could go get our first oil and gas field trial. Wait, you had billboards targeting one guy? So what we did, there was one guy at this saltwater disposal company who controlled all of the chemical spend. What we figured out was where he frequented—like he actually goes to the field, where he goes and where he lives, or at least the neighborhood he lives in. And we bought up the billboards. Billboards are cheap along the highway that he commutes to work on, so that every day as he's commuting to work he's just seeing your billboard. And then eventually he gets a call from us. It's like a priming aspect of it that we knew this was the guy. I was willing to spend 10 to 15k just to make sure that we can get in front of the guy. And the second that he saw that and got a call from us, he was like, "Oh I see your billboards everywhere." And so that's when he felt special, right? And this goes back to the customer experience. Do you think the CEO of Dow would do that? No, no way. They're not gonna do that. Soon Solugen had signed enough customers that they were able to raise money again and build their first state-of-the-art plant, the Bioforge 1. So this is Bioforge. Everything you're looking at here was built in five locations simultaneously and then shipped here on trucks. We rented a crane for four months and just stacked it up like Lego. So all of the tan tanks are filled with corn syrup. That's our raw material. We are able to hold four rail cars of corn syrup at any one time, which is about 800,000 pounds. So it's like 800,000 pounds of corn syrup in these four large tan tanks. That's the starting material for the whole plant. This is the reactor that makes it happen. We feed in one Coke bottle of enzyme and you get two to four tanker trucks of product. That's how efficient the enzyme is. Wow. After filling up the tanks, Solugen loads up trucks to distribute the chemicals to nearby customers. So this is the end of the line. This is where customers pull up, either with their own trucks or Solugen supplies trucks and does all the logistics for them, and we're able to fill up trucks at about 300 gallons per minute. Building out lots of factories near customers to keep shipping costs down is a key part of how Solugen has managed to undercut its larger competitors. What has it been like to build new physical stuff in America? It is definitely challenging, but if you're in a part of the country that wants that manufacturing back, that's favorable to manufacturing, it is absolutely possible to build in America. What does Solugen look like in 10 more years? It's actually gonna be multiple different assets. We've taken enzymes and metal catalysts and applied them to not just Bioforges but other types of manufacturing assets that combine them. We'll be solving the most fascinating customer problems that right now we're not even aware of. Some of the problems that we're gonna solve, they don't exist yet. And so it's like just creating a culture that's willing to be wrong and solve those problems is actually what's most important right now.

Andrej Karpathy on Code Agents, AutoResearch, and the Loopy Era of AI

No Priors: AI, Machine Learning, Tech, & Startups(63.8K) ·66:32 ·172.7K views · 2026-03-20
Andrej Karpathy (AI Researcher)
Andrej Karpathy (formerly of OpenAI and Tesla) describes a fundamental shift in software engineering from manual coding to the orchestration of autonomous agents.
TL;DR Andrej Karpathy (formerly of OpenAI and Tesla) describes a fundamental shift in software engineering from manual coding to the orchestration of autonomous agents. He argues that the next phase of AI involves "AutoResearch"—removing the human bottleneck to allow agents to...

TL;DR

Andrej Karpathy (formerly of OpenAI and Tesla) describes a fundamental shift in software engineering from manual coding to the orchestration of autonomous agents. He argues that the next phase of AI involves "AutoResearch"—removing the human bottleneck to allow agents to recursively self-improve, conduct experiments, and manage complex systems through natural language and APIs.

The New Engineering Paradigm: From Coding to Orchestration

  • The "AI Psychosis": Karpathy describes a massive capability jump occurring around December 2023, where his workflow shifted from 80% manual coding to nearly 100% delegation to AI agents.
  • Macro Actions vs. Lines of Code: Mastery in the "loopy era" involves moving in macro actions (e.g., "implement this entire feature") rather than writing functions. Engineers now act as supervisors over parallelized agent "teams."
  • Token Throughput as the New Bottleneck: Just as PhD students once felt nervous when their GPUs weren’t running, Karpathy argues that modern developers should feel "nervous" when they aren't maximizing their "token throughput" or subscription quotas.
  • The Importance of Agent Persona: Karpathy highlights that an agent’s personality (e.g., Claude's "Soul MD") is not just flavor; it affects utility. High-performing agents should feel like teammates who offer "earned praise" rather than mindless sycophancy.

AutoResearch and Recursive Self-Improvement

  • Removing the Human Bottleneck: The goal of "AutoResearch" is to set an objective and metric, then let the agent run autonomously. Karpathy notes that his AutoResearch agent found better hyperparameters for his `nanogpt` project than he could achieve with 20 years of expertise.
  • The "Program.md" Concept: Karpathy views a research organization as a set of Markdown files describing roles and connections. In the future, agents will not only follow these instructions but optimize the "Program.md" itself to create more efficient research workflows.
  • Verifiable vs. Soft Domains: Agents excel in domains with objective, verifiable metrics (e.g., CUDA kernels, loss functions, unit tests). They struggle with "soft" tasks like humor or nuanced intent because these are outside the reach of current Reinforcement Learning (RL) feedback loops.

The Digital-Physical Divide and Robotics

  • Bits Move Faster Than Atoms: Karpathy predicts a massive "unhobbling" of the digital world because digital information can be manipulated at "light speed." Conversely, physical robotics will lag because manipulating matter is "a million times harder."
  • Agents as the "Glue" for Hardware: Karpathy's "Dobby" project (home automation) demonstrated how agents can bypass fragmented apps to control hardware (Sonos, HVAC, lights) directly via APIs and natural language, effectively "crumbling up" the need for bespoke UIs.
  • The Agentic Web: As agents run out of digital data to process, they will reach into the physical world via "sensors" (human contractors or IoT cameras) to conduct real-world experiments and return data to the "Borg."

The Future of Labor and "Speciation"

  • Jevons Paradox in Software: As AI makes software production cheaper, the demand for software will skyrocket rather than diminish. We are moving toward "ephemeral software" tailored to specific, momentary needs.
  • Model Speciation: While current labs favor a "monoculture" of general-purpose models, Karpathy expects "speciation" where smaller, specialized models are optimized for niche, high-value tasks like mathematics or specific coding languages.
  • The Shift in Education: Karpathy’s `microGPT` project (200 lines of pure algorithm) highlights a shift in learning. Instead of explaining concepts to humans, experts will explain concepts to agents, who then customize the explanation for the human student with "infinite patience."

Notable Quotes

"I don't think I've typed a line of code probably since December... I'm just in this state of psychosis of trying to figure out what's possible." — Andrej Karpathy
"You want to remove yourself as the bottleneck. You can't be there to prompt the next thing. The name of the game is: How can you get more agents running for longer periods of time without your involvement?" — Andrej Karpathy
"I simultaneously feel like I'm talking to an extremely brilliant PhD student who's been a systems programmer for their entire life, and a 10-year-old. This jaggedness is really strange." — Andrej Karpathy
"I don't think like a normal person actually realizes that this happened or how dramatic it was. The default workflow of building software is completely different as of basically December." — Andrej Karpathy

Like what was the motivation behind AutoResearch? AutoResearch, yeah. So I think like I had a tweet earlier where I kind of like said something along the lines of to get the most out of the tools that have become available now, you have to remove yourself as the—as the bottleneck. You can't be there to prompt the next thing. You—you need to take yourself outside. You have to arrange things such that they are completely autonomous. And so I kind of mentioned that the name of the game now is to increase your leverage. I put in just very few tokens just once in a while, and a huge amount of stuff happens on my behalf. And so AutoResearch, like I tweeted that and I think people liked it and whatnot, but—but they haven't like maybe worked through like the implications of that. And for me AutoResearch is an example of like an implication of that. Where it's like I don't want to be like the researcher in loop like looking at results etc. Like I'm—I'm holding the system back. So, the question is how do I refactor all the abstractions so that I'm not—I have to arrange it once and hit go. The name of the game is how can you get more agents running for longer periods of time without your involvement doing stuff on your behalf. And AutoResearch is just, yeah, here's an objective, here's a metric, here's your boundaries of what you can and cannot do. And go. And, yeah, it worked. You were surprised at its effectiveness. Yeah, I—I didn't expect it to work because—so I have the project NanoGPT. And fundamentally like I think a lot of people were very confused with my obsession for like training GPT-2 models and so on. But for me training GPT-2 models and so on is just a little harness, a little playground for training LLMs. And fundamentally what I'm more interested in is like this idea of recursive self-improvement and to what extent you can actually have LLMs improving LLMs. Because I think all the frontier labs, this is like the thing, for obvious reasons. And they're all trying to recursively self-improve roughly speaking. And so for me this is kind of like a little playpen of that. And I guess I like tuned NanoGPT already quite a bit by hand. In a good old fashioned way that I'm used to. Like I'm a researcher, I've done this for like, you know, two decades. I have some amount of, like—What is the opposite of hubris? Yeah. Earned confidence. Okay. I have like two decades of like, oh I've trained this model like thousands of times of like, so I've done a bunch of experiments, I've done hyperparameter tuning, I've done all the things I'm very used to and I've done for two decades. And I've gotten to a certain point, and I thought it was like fairly well tuned. And then I let AutoResearch go for like overnight and it came back with like tunings that I didn't see. And yeah, I did forget like the weight decay on the value embeddings and my Adam betas were not sufficiently tuned, and these things jointly interact. So like once you tune one thing, the other things have to potentially change too. You know, I shouldn't be a bottleneck, I shouldn't be running these hyperparameter search optimizations, I shouldn't be looking at the results. There's objective criteria in this case. So you just—you just have to arrange it so that it can just go forever. So that's a—a single sort of version of AutoResearch of like a single loop trying to improve. And I was surprised that it—it found these things that I, you know, the repo's already fairly well tuned and still found something. And that's just a single—it's a single loop. Amy Webb on the forces transforming society right now | Masters of Scale

Masters of Scale(153.0K) ·29:35 ·284 views · 2026-03-20
Amy Webb (Futurist and CEO, Future Today Strategy Group)
Futurist Amy Webb argues that tracking isolated "trends" is no longer sufficient in a fast-moving world; leaders must instead focus on "convergences"—the collision of multiple technologies and socio-political forces—to navigate an era of "creative de...
TL;DR Futurist Amy Webb argues that tracking isolated "trends" is no longer sufficient in a fast-moving world; leaders must instead focus on "convergences"—the collision of multiple technologies and socio-political forces—to navigate an era of "creative destruction" and avoid...

TL;DR

Futurist Amy Webb argues that tracking isolated "trends" is no longer sufficient in a fast-moving world; leaders must instead focus on "convergences"—the collision of multiple technologies and socio-political forces—to navigate an era of "creative destruction" and avoid the trap of short-term corporate thinking.

The Shift from Static Trends to "Convergences"

  • Creative Destruction: Webb cites economist Joseph Schumpeter to explain her decision to "kill" her famous annual trend report. She argues that old structures must be destroyed to make way for the new, and a static, 1,000-page report is no longer relevant in an era of rapid change.
  • Defining Convergence: Unlike a single trend (e.g., "cool badges" on social media), a convergence is the collision of multiple trends with large-scale external forces like government intervention, war, or economic shifts.
      • Redistributing Power: Convergences are "kingmakers" that rewrite rules, collapse old markets, and start new ones.
      • The "Shiny Object" Trap: Leaders often miss convergences because they are distracted by "the shiny"—temporary fads—and only realize a fundamental shift has occurred when it is too late to adapt.

Three Critical Modern Convergences

  • The Post-Search Internet: The era of Google’s search monopoly is ending.
      • Behavioral Change: Webb uses the example of her 80-year-old father-in-law, who now uses ChatGPT for all inquiries rather than searching on his Google Pixel phone.
      • Strategic Blindness: Companies reliant on traditional search traffic and ad models are facing a "long horizon" threat they are currently unequipped to handle.
  • Programmable Biology: The intersection of AI and "generative biology" allows biology to be editable and writable.
      • Impact Beyond Medicine: This shifts how we approach construction (e.g., self-healing concrete), agriculture, and energy.
      • Security Risks: It also opens the door to decentralized, lethal bioweapons.
  • The Corporate Panopticon: While China utilizes a top-down government surveillance state, the West has developed a decentralized "corporate panopticon."
      • Ambient Surveillance: Consumers are "ambiently surveyed" by millions of entities through a billion sensors.
      • The Convenience Trade-off: Society accepts this loss of privacy in exchange for loyalty points, spam filters, and banking convenience without understanding the long-term governance implications.

The Crisis of Short-Termism in the West

  • Unhinged Capitalism: Webb argues that U.S. companies are making "structurally stupid" decisions to juice short-term margins and share prices, often at the expense of long-term survival.
      • Misaligned Incentives: Boards often favor CFOs as CEOs because they represent "small-C" conservative stability, but these leaders are often not natural growth innovators.
  • The China Leapfrog: Webb highlights a dangerous disparity in national strategy:
      • U.S. Approach: Focused on "Free R&D"—high-level innovation without a structural plan for societal integration.
      • China’s Approach: Executing a 10-year infrastructure plan to ensure 1.4 billion people are actively using AI and automation in daily life, education, and industry.
  • The Strategic Vacuum: There is currently no comprehensive U.S. government plan to address the digital divide or prepare the education system for the AI era, potentially allowing China to "leapfrog" the U.S. within a decade.

Advice for Future-Proof Leadership

  • Productive Wandering: To imagine the future, leaders must allow their minds to wander and make connections between disparate data points, rather than relying solely on automated tools.
  • Response vs. Reaction: Webb urges leaders to slow down. While the world feels like it is moving at a head-spinning pace, reacting to every "shiny object" prevents the thoughtful response required for long-term success.
  • Business Model Innovation: Companies often treat the business model as an afterthought to the technology. Real success requires innovating the model *alongside* the tech, as seen in the historical case of Norwegian news organization Schibsted, which pioneered digital ad systems before its competitors.
"Convergences... redistribute power, they rewrite rules, they collapse markets, they start markets; they are kingmakers in a way they are the fundamental representation of creative destruction." — Amy Webb
"We have hit a moment in the United States where we have a form of capitalism that has gone almost to an extreme level, and I actually think this is potentially damaging companies... making structural, stupid short-term decisions to get that margin." — Amy Webb
"The world is not actually moving as fast as you feel like it is... you kind of have to learn how to respond thoughtfully versus react in the moment." — Amy Webb

Chris Pilkerton - Trump, tariffs and the trajectory of trade

Chatham House(89.4K) ·43:11 ·511 views · 2026-03-20
Chris Pilkerton (Assistant Secretary for Investment Security, US Treasury Department)
The United States is balancing an "open for business" economic posture with an increasingly rigorous national security screening framework for both inbound and outbound investments.
TL;DR The United States is balancing an "open for business" economic posture with an increasingly rigorous national security screening framework for both inbound and outbound investments. While maintaining the US as the premier destination for global capital, the Treasury...

TL;DR

The United States is balancing an "open for business" economic posture with an increasingly rigorous national security screening framework for both inbound and outbound investments. While maintaining the US as the premier destination for global capital, the Treasury Department is expanding its oversight into nascent technologies—specifically AI, quantum, and semiconductors—through modernized CFIUS processes and the upcoming implementation of the COINS Act.

The America First Investment Policy and CFIUS at 50

  • Dual Mandate: Under the "America First Investment Policy," the US seeks to remain the world's primary destination for foreign capital while ensuring national security remains "job number one."
  • Committee Structure: CFIUS (Committee on Foreign Investment in the United States) is celebrating its 50th year. It comprises nine member agencies—including Treasury, Energy, War, and Commerce—chaired by Treasury Secretary Bessent.
      • Expertise-Driven: Pilkerton describes the multi-agency approach as "iron sharpens iron," utilizing diverse expertise to identify risks in technology transfers and data privacy.
  • Process Efficiency: A primary focus is providing predictability and certainty for investors, particularly small businesses with "short runways" that require fast regulatory decisions to flourish.

Inbound Investment: Mitigation and Enforcement

  • Risk Identification: Primary national security concerns for inbound deals typically involve sensitive technology transfers and the protection of private citizen data.
  • Mitigation Strategies: When risks are identified, CFIUS works with parties to implement "time-tested" safeguards, such as:
      • Governance Controls: Proxy voting trusts or requiring a dedicated security officer/monitor within the company.
      • Compliance Audits: On-the-ground auditors and regular reporting to ensure ongoing adherence to security agreements.
  • Non-Notified Program: The Treasury maintains a team to investigate transactions that were not voluntarily reported.
      • Presidential Intervention: Pilkerton noted that the President blocked several cases this year that were discovered through the non-notified program after the deals had already closed.
  • Scale and Trajectory: While smaller businesses may initially present lower risks, the "dual-use" potential of their technology or significant scaling (similar to the trajectory of TikTok) can trigger renewed jurisdiction and scrutiny.

Outbound Investment and the COINS Act

  • Outbound Controls: A newer regime (codified in 2023-2024) focuses on a "prohibition and notification mechanism" for US investments moving into China.
      • Sector Focus: Current controls target AI, quantum computing, and semiconductors.
      • Beyond Capital: The concern is not just the money, but the transfer of talent networks and intangible support that could help a competitor weaponize nascent technologies.
  • The COINS Act: Legislative updates from late 2025 will be implemented through 2026, becoming fully live by Q1 2027.
      • Expanded Scope: The Act adds "hypersonics" and "supercomputing" to the restricted sectors and grants the Treasury Secretary authority to add more sectors as technology evolves.
      • Broader Geography: It will expand the list of "countries of destination" subject to these controls.

Modernizing the Review Process: The Known Investor Program

  • The Pilot Phase: Treasury is currently piloting a "Known Investor Program" with a handful of "frequent filers" and funds.
  • Operational Efficiency: By conducting deep-dive due diligence on the investors themselves ahead of time, the Committee can focus primarily on the specifics of the transaction during a live filing.
  • Public Rollout: The program is expected to be open to the broader investment community in early 2027, aimed at reducing the need for "withdrawals and refiles" and shortening transaction timelines.

International Cooperation and the Five Eyes

  • The Special Relationship: Pilkerton emphasized the "Accepted Foreign States" status provided to Five Eyes partners (including the UK), which streamlines certain types of investment reviews.
  • Global Technical Assistance: The US actively shares best practices and operational "know-how" with allies to help them build their own investment screening regimes.
  • Closing Security Gaps: Strong screening regimes in allied nations are considered a US national security priority to prevent "bad actors" from using partner economies as a "bad vector" to infiltrate the US market.

***

Notable Quotes

"National security is sort of job number one when it comes through the CFIUS process, but we want to be able to engage with companies and investors to ensure we’re sharing information and talking best practices." — Chris Pilkerton
"In a non-notified context, you can’t protect against what you don’t know about." — Chris Pilkerton
"It's one of those circumstances where iron sharpens iron... you have expertise and skills from folks that have been inside and outside of government really talking about what that national security risk might be." — Chris Pilkerton

So I've seen firsthand how small businesses need that level of certainty to operate. And because they're small, their runways are usually pretty short. Well, large companies certainly have their own runways and they need that same predictability and certainty, and that's what we're trying to bring to the role and I think we've been successful in that. Another thing that the America First Investment Policy talks about is that national security role. So how does that process work? The Committee on Foreign Investment in the United States is made up of nine member agencies. And it's a lot of the larger agencies that you might imagine. So obviously, Treasury—Secretary Bessent is the chair. You have the Department of Energy, the Department of War, the Department of Commerce, and a number of other sort of key area agencies or sector area agencies that have expertise that are shared amongst the entire committee. And it's one of those circumstances where iron sharpens iron, right? So you have expertise, you have skills from folks that have been inside and outside of government, really talking about what that national security risk might be. And so a typical national security risk that we might see in say a technology deal is potentially something around privacy data, or something around tech transfer. And then we work with the parties to come to a level of mitigation. And then ultimately, that mitigation moves into compliance where our team oversees that investment, oversees that capital, and oversees that process. And we have a couple of different programs right now. One is actually available and has been in place for a little over a year; one's called the outbound program. So the CFIUS piece of it that I was mentioning before covers the inbound program. The outbound program was part of an executive order from 2023. And as of last year, it was actually codified into law. We've had about a year of practice with this order. And what it basically does is it creates a prohibition and notification mechanism on certain investments going into China. And it focuses on three technologies: AI, quantum, and semiconductors. We've had about a year to implement this program and we've worked really closely with the industries and folks that are looking in this space. And that's really important because not only do they learn more from us, but we learn more from them. That level of engagement is really critical for the role that I currently hold. The COINS Act was the legislative piece from late 2025. And that'll be implemented through the course of rules and regulation this year, and probably become live as a regime in the end of Q1 of 2027. The significant difference is that it adds additional countries of origin, or rather of destination, for that space, but it also adds additional sectors. It adds hypersonics and supercomputing. And it also gives the Secretary of the Treasury the ability to add sectors as well. So we'll be beginning that rulemaking process later on this year, and we anticipate getting a lot of public feedback in large part because both we and the public have been working under this regime. So we anticipate a lot of lessons learned. Another area that's part of the America First Investment Policy is something called the Known Investor Program. When you think about a transaction, there's two parts to it: the actual parties that are transacting it and the transaction itself. And when we vet a transaction, we've got to look at the whole thing. So this came out of the America First Investment Policy, and what we've done is started a pilot this year where we're reviewing a handful of funds and investors. And when I say review, there's a lot of questions and answers that we've actually sent out to them. Many of them are what we call frequent filers, so they have engaged in our process before. And we do a due diligence, we do a vet, and then ultimately they would receive this status. So like I said, we're in the pilot now, and I think that will give us the opportunity for when they come through with cases to be able to focus on the transaction itself, recognizing that there will be a refresh of the due diligence as well. So we're excited for that to hopefully be open to the public in the early part of 2027. The last thing I wanted to mention in my opening remarks is the level of international engagement we have. So as I've mentioned, we've just been here for a couple of days, but we've had the great opportunity to meet with the UK government and sort of talk about best practices and a variety of things that are on both of our plates. And we have a team that is consistently doing bilateral engagement really all around the world. Now, because CFIUS has been around for 50 years, we've learned a few things along the way. And we're excited to share that with a lot of different countries. And of course, we're sharing the principles, but the other piece of it is the actual operations. You know, how do you do it? How do you move cases along? And how do you engage with industry to ensure that you're taking care of job number one, which is national security, but still maintain that open for business environment? So, as I mentioned at the top, very excited to be here today, very excited to be in the UK, and I look forward to any questions you might have. Super. Chris, thank you very much. If we could sort of take inbound first and then outbound second. Is it that you aim to be fully consistent with a new investment, or is there somehow a different approach when it's a homegrown thing, if you like? Sure. So I can't speak to any specific cases. There is confidentiality associated with the CFIUS process. But I think it sort of goes back to first principles. What is CFIUS there to do? It's there to engage and assess the national security risks. And in a lot of ways, CFIUS is sort of the last resort. I'm sure many of the businesses or investors who are here today have gone through the use of export controls or the Department of War's FOCI mitigation. And those are all components that not only do we engage with, but as I mentioned at the top, they're represented in the transaction. So we look specifically at what those national security risks might be for the particular transaction. And then when we're looking to mitigate, we have time-tested components of things that may be more helpful for a larger company, right? So for example, there may be something where, you know, you have a proxy voting trust or something of that nature. You might need a monitor or a security person at the company. So these are all things that we've seen, these are all things that we've done, and these are all things that we actually have a process to not only implement, but then to continue and monitor. And this is all a conversation with the company, right? Because in order for us to be able to clear an actual case, we need to make sure that the company is on board and they're committed to it. And it's a great question because sometimes, you know, we will get information about cases where we may have jurisdiction but they don't go through the CFIUS process. We have a whole team that's called the non-notified program. And those cases can be more difficult as you might imagine, because the cases have already closed. And so in order to implement, you know, some sort of reactive kind of mitigation approach may be difficult. The President has blocked a few cases this year, as is his authority under CFIUS for national security concerns. And those cases were all non-notified cases. So, part of it as well, as I was mentioning, is that we want businesses to be able to talk to us. And even if you have to do it on an anonymous, no-names basis, it's really important that we have those conversations if there is any confusion at all. Because when businesses and investors are going into those negotiations, to be able to factor in or be able to bake in some of the information that we're providing is something that's really important for the company, but it's also important for the CFIUS program. But, but I am right that, you know, basically scale can change an entity from being not an issue to being an issue. Is that right? Or even you may have an entity that sort of initially wasn't doing certain things and then started doing certain things, and again it becomes an issue when it previously wasn't. Is that right? Right. Well it's about the investment and about where we have jurisdiction. Now, I think I would certainly agree that maybe if you have a smaller business, there may be less risks to address. But there may be risks to address nonetheless, right? If you have a larger business, there are additional components or things that we may want to address. But if there's a particular technology that has foreign investment that's coming into the company, you know, the potential dual use for that technology as an example may be a reason that we have to mitigate that transaction. Okay. So let's look at the outbound aspect now, which is, as you said, newer and indeed new legislation and new experience being developed. I mean, one of the questions really is, you know, there are all kinds of other controls preventing sensitive technology going to countries that you'd be concerned about. Why do you need, if you like, a financing control on top of that? What is the element that you are concerned about? And, in a way, a reason to be particularly concerned, as soon as you get into financing you're opening up a whole new area of potential controls, which generally a country like the US would not be keen on. Capital controls are generally not consistent with the US view of the world. So, can you explain the logic for getting into outbound financing controls on top of what you already have? Sure. So when you look at the Treasury Department, there's really sort of three components to it. There's the international program where we fall, and that includes us and the trade and tariff teams. There is the domestic banking team, and then there's the terrorism and finance area and that includes OFAC, the sanctions area, Financial Crimes Enforcement Network, money laundering or what have you. I use that as an example because when you look across the entire US government, there are different authorities that are part of this whole sort of large web of regulation that focus on particularly sensitive areas. So Commerce controls the export list as well, just as an example. What the outbound program does is, when we think about some of these nascent technologies for example and the opportunities for them to be weaponized against the United States and their allies, you think about if that investment is going into China. It's not just about that investment. It's also about the talent network; it's about the support that may come from that investment. And so when these actual areas of sort of indigenous technology development are occurring, we want to make sure that we have a chance to review that and ensure that the national security risks are minimized. So then moving to the sort of relationship with US allies. I mean, this is kind of relevant both on the inward and on the outward. So, for example, you may have an investment let's say from a UK business, which is primarily a UK business, but which has some, let's say, some Chinese investment in it. Now from the UK's investment screening point of view, they may have looked at it and said, "We think this is fine because all the mitigations are in place." And indeed, actually, in a world where China has in some areas better technology than the rest of the world, you may actually positively want to attract Chinese technology into your economy. I mean I'm thinking particularly in areas like some of the green tech areas and so on—electric vehicles and so on. So how does the US approach that situation? I mean are you willing to take the UK authorities' judgment as to whether that is an investment one needs to be concerned about or not? Because that company may then want to invest in the US, and so are you going to look through, or just say it's UK, it's okay, or are you going to look through and say actually we're worried about...? How does this work? So, for a UK-based program or investment, just UK, we have something called Accepted Foreign States. And that is a status that is provided to the Five Eyes countries for certain types of investments. And we have that, and that's been a great relationship; the UK is a part of that, obviously. When you talk about a scenario that you're talking about, you know, we talked about outbound before with respect to the investments in China. With respect to inbound, so the sort of more traditional CFIUS lane, the program is country agnostic. So when we're looking at investors or investment, we're doing our due diligence across the board. Particularly when other countries, as you know, are very, very cautious about outbound investment screening. Sure. Are We In The Middle Of A Global Economic Collapse?

The Cārvāka Podcast(184.0K) ·159:03 ·18.7K views · 2026-03-20
Kushal Mehra (Host, The Cārvāka Podcast) Balaji Srinivasan (Entrepreneur and Investor)

TL;DR The destruction of Middle Eastern oil infrastructure marks a terminal collapse of the post-war "Petrodollar" era, signaling a global shift away from U.S. hegemony toward a multipolar world dominated by "civilization states" like India, China, and Russia. The "Abrahamic...

TL;DR

The destruction of Middle Eastern oil infrastructure marks a terminal collapse of the post-war "Petrodollar" era, signaling a global shift away from U.S. hegemony toward a multipolar world dominated by "civilization states" like India, China, and Russia.

The Collapse of the Global Energy Order

  • The "Abrahamic Artery" Rupture: The blowing up of oil and gas infrastructure in the Middle East is a humanitarian and economic crisis unrivaled in modern history.
      • Long-term Damage: Strategic assets, such as Qatar’s LNG trains, may take three to five years to rebuild, meaning disruptions are indefinite, not temporary.
      • Petrodollar Death: The U.S. dollar was historically backed by military protection of oil. With the U.S. unable to secure the Gulf, the fundamental value proposition of the dollar is evaporating.
  • Supply Chain Choke Points: The Strait of Hormuz remains the world’s most critical vulnerability.
      • LNG Crisis: India receives 46% of its LNG from Qatar; current damage has already led to force majeure notices and a 10-20% cut in domestic gas use.
  • Shift to Autarky: Nations must now pivot toward "Maslow’s Hierarchy" of needs—securing food and energy locally rather than relying on global trade.

The Decline of the American "Security Umbrella"

  • Bases as Liabilities: Historically, a U.S. military base signaled protection and investment. Today, these bases are "sitting ducks" within range of Iranian and proxy missiles.
      • Failed Deterrence: The U.S. cannot protect its GCC (Gulf Cooperation Council) allies from asymmetric warfare (cheap drones vs. expensive interceptors).
  • Internal U.S. Fragmentation: The U.S. is no longer a unified actor but a collection of "disunited tribes."
      • IQ and Competence Drift: Balaji notes a secular decline in U.S. institutional IQ and reading levels, paired with a radical ascent in national debt.
      • The "Bald Eagle" Metaphor: The U.S. operates as a predator that bombs from a distance and retreats to its "nest" in North America, leaving allies to manage the resulting "Trashcanistan" (regional chaos).
  • Digital Secession: Polarization has reached a point where "ideology became biology," with Red and Blue Americans no longer mating, socialized together, or sharing a common economy.

The Rise of Civilization States

  • The New Eurasian Quartet: The world is reorganizing into four major spheres of influence:

1. Russia: Dominating Northern Asia.

2. China: Dominating East Asia and manufacturing.

3. India: Dominating South Asia through "Third Way" diplomacy.

4. Greater Iran: Emerging as the dominant power in West Asia (Middle East).

  • Iran’s Sophistication: Contrary to Western stereotypes, Iran is a "civilization state" with high-IQ populations and a decentralized, resilient command structure. They have prepared for autarkic survival for 40 years.
  • China’s Victory: China has effectively won the trade war, dominating shipbuilding, drones, rare earths, and electricity production. They are now positioning themselves as the "adult in the room" for global free trade.

Strategic Imperatives for India

  • Diplomacy as a Superweapon: India’s strength lies in its ability to negotiate with all sides (Russia, U.S., Iran, China) without being sucked into a "suicide bomber" alliance like Ukraine.
  • Energy Independence (Autarky):
      • Nuclear & Coal: India must double down on domestic coal-to-chemicals and small modular nuclear reactors.
      • Solar Tipping Point: Rapid scaling of solar infrastructure is necessary to offset the loss of Middle Eastern hydrocarbons.
  • Bureaucratic Acceleration: Kushal Mehra argues that the "speed of reform" is India’s greatest bottleneck.
      • Defense Shield: India needs to accelerate its "Sudarshan Chakra" defensive systems to protect critical infrastructure.
      • Red Tape: The government must stop "inter-departmental warfare" in courts and simplify processes like the OCI (Overseas Citizenship of India) to facilitate a "brain gain" as the diaspora flees Western instability.

Notable Quotes

"The U.S. military base is now a liability, not an asset. The U.S. basically wants you to die first... it’s not going to protect you; you’re going to become a target." — Balaji Srinivasan
"The problem doesn’t lie in the intent of the government. The problem lies in the speed at which the government is able to pull these things off, and that’s just not good enough." — Kushal Mehra
"The American Empire is like a great-grandson who inherited a factory he could never build. He cashes the checks but doesn’t know how to operate the machinery when the market shifts." — Balaji Srinivasan
"In this scenario, India’s number one goal is: stay out of war, stay out of war, stay out of war. Minimum necessary conflict, maximum feasible diplomacy." — Balaji Srinivasan

MAGA Civil War Erupts Over Iran | Pivot

Pivot with Kara Swisher and Scott Galloway(229.0K) ·74:15 ·165.1K views · 2026-03-20

TL;DR The geopolitical landscape is destabilizing as oil prices surge toward $120/barrel amid tensions with Iran, while a shifting AI power dynamic sees Anthropic aggressively seizing enterprise market share from a distracted and over-extended OpenAI. Energy Markets: Oil prices...

TL;DR

The geopolitical landscape is destabilizing as oil prices surge toward $120/barrel amid tensions with Iran, while a shifting AI power dynamic sees Anthropic aggressively seizing enterprise market share from a distracted and over-extended OpenAI.

Geopolitical Volatility and the "Coalition of One"

  • Energy Markets: Oil prices spiked to over $119 per barrel following attacks on energy sites in the Gulf.
      • Economic Impact: Every dollar increase at the pump costs middle-income families roughly $530 annually; low-income families already spend 20% of their income on energy.
  • Diplomatic Friction: President Trump has demanded allies send warships to the Strait of Hormuz, receiving what analysts call a "global raspberry" as NATO allies distance themselves from unilateral U.S. actions.
  • Defense Spending: The Pentagon has requested $200 billion to fund potential conflict with Iran. Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth defended the figure, stating "it takes money to kill bad guys."
  • MAGA Infighting: Kara Swisher predicts an escalation in the "micropenis war"—crude, public infighting between figures like Megyn Kelly, Marjorie Taylor Greene, and Donald Trump. Scott Galloway notes that algorithms financially incentivize this "vile and incendiary" behavior.

The Silicon Valley "Introspection" Debate

  • Marc Andreessen Critique: The Netscape founder recently claimed his goal is "zero introspection," dismissing therapy and self-reflection as manufactured 20th-century concepts.
      • Historical Counter-point: Scott Galloway argues that the greatest minds (Einstein, Oppenheimer) and business leaders (Bill Gates) were deeply introspective regarding the ramifications of their work.
      • The "Anti-Hero" Trend: Swisher describes this lack of introspection as a "dystopian" far-right performance of masculinity that ignores the human element of leadership.
  • Mark Cuban as Contrast: Swisher contrasts Andreessen’s "soulless" approach with Mark Cuban’s recent focus on "Cost Plus" drugs and social value, describing Cuban as a leader who has "progressed" while others have moved "backwards."

The State of AI: OpenAI vs. Anthropic

  • Enterprise Shift: Anthropic is currently dominating the enterprise AI market, capturing 73% of new spending compared to OpenAI’s 27%.
      • Market Velocity: Anthropic’s annual recurring revenue (ARR) surged to $19 billion, while OpenAI remains stagnant at roughly $20 billion.
  • OpenAI Focus: Internal pressure has forced OpenAI to scale back "do everything" strategies to focus on coding and business users.
  • Prediction - The End of Sora: Scott Galloway predicts OpenAI will soon shut down its Sora social media app.
      • High Burn, Low Return: Sora reportedly costs $15 million a day to operate while generating less than $500,000 per month.
      • Public Sentiment: 70% of global consumers report being uncomfortable with fully AI-generated creative content.

Corporate Governance and the Federal Reserve

  • Jerome Powell’s Standoff: Fed Chair Jerome Powell intends to stay in his post until a successor is confirmed, despite DOJ investigations pushed by Trump allies like Jeanine Pirro.
      • Legislative Gridlock: Senator Tom Tillis is blocking the confirmation of Trump’s nominee, Kevin Warsh, effectively leaving Powell in control.
  • Meta’s $70 Billion Failure: Despite Meta’s attempts to walk back rumors of the Metaverse’s death, Galloway maintains the project is "on the green mile," having burned $70 billion in capital for a product that makes users "nauseous."
  • Disney’s Succession: Bob Iger has officially named Josh D’Amaro as his successor.
      • Performance Review: Galloway criticizes Iger’s second tenure, noting Disney’s stock has underperformed relative to Netflix (0% return vs. 600% for Netflix) over the last decade.
